بالواو وفي «أو» بعده بـ «أو» يقع بعد غد قوله: (ولو قال إن تزوجت زينب قبيل عمرة الخ) انظر لما يأتي عن التتمة قبيل قوله «أنا منك طالق لغو». 
للشرط غير مقيد بالقبلية ولحكم الشرع لأن النصوص ناطقة بشرعية الطلاق، وهذا يؤدي إلى رفعها فيتفرع في المسألة المذكورة وقوع ثلاث الواحدة المنجزة وثنتان من المعلقة. ولو طلقها ثنتين وقعتا وواحدة من المعلقة أو طلقها ثلاثاً يقعن فينزل الطلاق المعلق لا يصادف أهلية فيلغو، ولو كان قال إن طلقتك فأنت طالق قبله ثم طلقها واحدة وقعت ثنتان المنجزة والمعلقة، وقس على ذلك. كذا في فتح القدير وفيه نظر، لأنه ينتقض بقوله تعالى وما بكم من نعمة فمن الله ﴾ [النحل : ٥٣] فإن الأول استقرار النعمة بالمخاطبين، والثاني كونها من عز وجل وليس الأول سبباً للثاني بل الأول فرع للثاني. وقال الرضى: لا يلزم مع الفاء أن يكون الأول سبباً للثاني بل اللازم أن يكون ما بعد الفاء لازماً لمضمون ما قبلها كما في جميع صور الشرط والجزاء، ففي قوله تعالى وما بكم من نعمة فمن الله كون النعمة منه لازم حصولها معنى، ولا يغرنك قول بعضهم إن الشرط سبب في الجزاء اهـ. وتمامه في شرح المغني للدماميني من بحث ما من المبحث الأول. وحينئذ فلا يلغو قوله «قبله» لعدم المنافاة ولا يضر رفع شرعية الطلاق على واحد اختار لنفسه ذلك فألزم نفسه به كما لو قال كلما تزوجت امرأة فهي طالق فإنه صحيح عندنا وإن كان فيه سد باب النكاح المشروع. وفي القنية من آخر كتاب الأيمان قال لها كلما وقع عليك طلاقي فأنت قبله طالق ثلاثاً ثم طلقها بعد
النحاة يفصح عن
قوله (بالوقوع) أي وقوع الثلاث كما هو مقتضى التفريع ويأتي التصريح به أيضاً في كلامه، وسنذكر عن ابن حجر الخلاف في وقوع المنجز وحده ووقوع الثلاث قوله (لأن الإيقاع في الماضي إيقاع في الحال الظاهر أنه تعليل للقول الأول بالوقوع ، وقوله ونقول أيضاً الخ» تأييد له فأخر تعليل القول الأول إلى ما بعد القولين ليرتبط الكلام قوله وفيه نظر لأنه ينتقض الخ) منع لقوله ولحكم العقل. وقوله بعده ولا يضر رفع شرعية الطلاق الخ منع لقوله والحكم الشرع». قال في النهر بعد ذكره الحاصل كلام المؤلف وفيه نظر من وجهين الأول ما قاله الرضي إنما هو مذهب ذلك ما في المطول لا نسلم أن الشرط النحوي ما يتوقف عليه وجود الشيء بل هو المذكور بعد إن وأخواته معلق عليه حصول مضمون الجزاء أي حكم بأنه يحصل مضمون تلك الجملة عند حصوله فهو في الغالب ملزوم والجزاء لازم وانتفاء اللازم يوجب انتفاء الملزوم من غير عكس. ثم قال : الشرط عندهم أعم من أن يكون سبباً نحو «لو كانت الشمس طالعة فالعالم مضيء» أو شرطاً نحو لو كان لي مال لحججت أو غيرهما نحو «لو كان النهار موجوداً لكانت الشمس طالعة. الثاني سلمنا أن أداة الشرط لا يلزم أن تكون سبباً لكن بطلان تقدم الشيء على شرطه ضروري لأنه موقوف عليه فلا يحصل قبله كما في التلويح وفيه الحق أن بطلان تقدم الشيء على شرطه أظهر من بطلان تقدمه على السبب الجواز أن يثبت بأسباب شتى .اهـ. وبهذا يبطل قوله «فلا يلغو» قوله قبله لعدم المنافاة اهـ. قلت: لا يخفى عليك أن أول هذين الوجهين مؤيد لكلام المؤلف في دعواه عدم لزوم كون مدخول أداة الشرط سبباً والجزاء مسبباً عنه إذ لا خفاء أن المراد هنا بالشرط
Book of Divorce, Chapter of Divorce. (Page 473) Nonsense: If he married her before yesterday, it occurs now. 'You are divorced as long as I have not divorced you' or 'whenever I do not divorce' or 'whenever I do not divorce you.' Al-Jami': It is not said that his statement 'before I marry you' is nonsense and that there is a separation between the condition and the conditioned, so that divorce should not be attached to marriage. For we say: We do not concede that it is nonsense; rather it is a clarification of what the beginning of the speech implied, because it necessitates that it is an immediate pronouncement by inserting the existence of his statement described as being before marriage. So it becomes as if he said to his wife: 'You are divorced this hour if you enter the house,' or 'You are divorced before you enter the house if you enter the house,' because his statement 'this hour' and 'before you enter' clarifies what the beginning of the speech required, although if he had made a separation there, it would be immediate. Here, if he made 'before I marry you' a separation, it would be nonsense, so it is more appropriate to consider it non-separating to validate the speech of a sane person. In al-Muhit: If he said, 'If I marry so-and-so after so-and-so, they are both divorced,' and he married them as stated, they are divorced because he attached divorce to marrying them. Because his statement 'after so-and-so' means after marrying so-and-so, so marrying so-and-so becomes mentioned necessarily, and he married them as conditioned, so the condition is found and divorce occurs. If he said, 'If I marry so-and-so before so-and-so, they are both divorced,' and he married the first, she is divorced because the condition regarding her is found, which is precedence, because describing something with precedence does not require the existence of what comes after. If he marries the second, she is also divorced. It is said: She should not be divorced. If he said, 'If I marry Zaynab before Amrah by a month, they are both divorced,' and he married Zaynab, then Amrah after her by a month, Zaynab is divorced immediately due to the existence of the condition, and it is not retroactive, as if he said, 'You are divorced before the arrival of so-and-so by a month.' Amrah is not divorced because he attached Amrah's divorce to a month before her marriage. If he said, 'If I marry Zaynab just before Amrah,' and he married Zaynab alone, she is not divorced because 'just before' is an expression for a subtle moment that connects to what follows it, and that is not known except by marrying Amrah, as if he said, 'You are divorced just before nightfall,' she is not divorced except at sunset. If he said 'before nightfall,' she is divorced immediately. If he marries Amrah after that, Zaynab is divorced, not Amrah. If the interval between the two marriages is long, neither is divorced. His statement: (If he married her before yesterday, it occurs now) because he attributed it to an incompatible state, and it cannot be validated as a report either, so it is an enactment, and enactment in the past is enactment in the present, so it occurs immediately. On this point, some later scholars among our masters ruled in the issue of al-dawr transmitted from later Shafi'is that it occurs, which is: If I divorce you, you are divorced before that three times. Most of them ruled that she is not divorced by executing her divorce because if it were executed, the suspended three would have occurred before it. The occurrence of three prior to the execution prevents the executed, because of the occurrence of the executed and the suspended. For enactment in the past is enactment in the present. We also say: This changes the ruling of language because the parts descend after the condition or with it, not before it, and also the ruling of reason because the object of the conditional particle is a cause and the result is an effect, and it is not reasonable for the effect to precede the cause. So his statement 'before it' is entirely nonsense; thus the divorce remains as the result of his statement 'you are divorced tomorrow' and after it with 'and', and in 'or' after it with 'or', it occurs after tomorrow. His statement: (If he said, 'If I marry Zaynab just before Amrah...') See what comes later from al-Tatimma about 'I am divorced from you is nonsense.' (Page 474) For the condition is not restricted by precedence, and by the ruling of the Shari'a because the texts speak of the legitimacy of divorce, and this leads to its abrogation. So from the mentioned issue, three occur: one executed and two from the suspended. If he divorced her two, they occur and one from the suspended; or if he divorced her three, they all occur, so the suspended divorce does not encounter eligibility and becomes nonsense. If he had said, 'If I divorce you, you are divorced before that,' then he divorced her once, two occur, the executed and the suspended, and analogize accordingly. Thus in Fath al-Qadir, but there is a critique because it is contradicted by His statement: 'And whatever blessing you have is from Allah' (16:53), for the first is the existence of blessing among the addressed, and the second is its being from Allah, and the first is not a cause for the second; rather the first is a branch of the second. Al-Radi said: It is not necessary with 'fa' that the first be a cause for the second; rather it is necessary that what is after 'fa' is a necessary consequence of the content of what precedes it, as in all forms of condition and result. So in His statement 'And whatever blessing you have is from Allah,' the blessing being from Him is a necessary consequence of its existence in meaning. Do not be deceived by the statement of some that the condition is a cause for the result. Its completion is in Sharh al-Mughni by al-Damamini from the discussion of 'ma' from the first discussion. Then his statement 'before it' is not nonsense due to lack of incompatibility, and it does not harm the legitimacy of divorce for one who chose that for himself and bound himself with it, as if he said, 'Every time I marry a woman, she is divorced,' which is valid with us even if it closes the door of legitimate marriage. In al-Qunyah from the end of the book of oaths: He said to her, 'Whenever my divorce falls upon you, you are before that divorced three times,' then he divorces her after... The grammarians clarify. His statement (by occurrence) means the occurrence of three, as required by the subdivision, and the declaration of that will also come in his speech. We will mention from Ibn Hajar the difference in the occurrence of the executed alone and the occurrence of three. His statement (because enactment in the past is enactment in the present) is apparently a justification for the first opinion of occurrence. His statement 'We also say...' supports it, so he delayed the justification of the first opinion until after the two opinions to connect the speech. His statement 'There is a critique because it is contradicted...' refutes his statement 'and by the ruling of reason'. His statement after that 'And it does not harm the legitimacy of divorce...' refutes his statement 'and by the ruling of the Shari'a'. In al-Nahr after mentioning the outcome of the author's speech: 'There is a critique from two aspects: first, what al-Radi said is only a madhhab; that in al-Mutawwal we do not concede that the grammatical condition is what the existence of something depends on; rather it is what is mentioned after 'in' and its sisters, upon which the occurrence of the content of the result is suspended, i.e., it is ruled that the content of that sentence occurs when it occurs. So it is mostly a necessary condition and the result is necessary, and the negation of the necessary necessitates the negation of the necessary condition without the converse. Then he said: The condition with them is more general than being a cause, like 'if the sun is shining, the world is illuminated,' or a condition like 'if I had money, I would perform Hajj,' or other like 'if the day existed, the sun would be shining.' Second, even if we concede that the conditional particle does not necessarily imply a cause, the invalidity of something preceding its condition is necessary because it depends on it, so it does not occur before it, as in al-Talwih. In it, the truth is that the invalidity of something preceding its condition is more apparent than the invalidity of its preceding the cause, because it is possible to be established by various causes. By this, his statement 'so it is not nonsense' is invalidated, i.e., his statement 'before it' due to lack of incompatibility. I say: It is not hidden from you that the first of these two aspects supports the author's statement in his claim that it is not necessary for the object of the conditional particle to be a cause and the result to be an effect, because it is clear that what is meant here by condition...
Source pages p. 473

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, addressing rulings of conditional divorce involving temporal clauses like 'before' and 'after'. The author first discusses the case where a man says to his wife, 'If I married you before yesterday, you are divorced now.' He explains that such a statement is valid as an immediate divorce because it is incompatible with a past time reference; it cannot be a report, so it becomes an enactment that takes effect immediately. The commentary from Al-Jami' adds that saying 'before I marry you' is not nonsense; rather it clarifies that the divorce is immediate, similar to saying 'you are divorced this hour if you enter the house.' The passage then presents various scenarios from Al-Muhit: conditional divorces involving two women with 'after' and 'before' clauses, and the distinction between 'qabl' (before) and 'qabil' (just before). The core legal issue is how to interpret temporal conditions in divorce pronouncements. The author then transitions to a famous problematic case known as 'al-dawr' (the circle), where a husband says, 'If I divorce you, you are divorced before that three times.' This creates a paradox: the suspended divorce cannot precede the executed one. The author presents the view of some later Hanafi scholars that all three divorces occur, while most rule that the executed divorce does not occur because the three suspended divorces would have already occurred, making the executed one impossible. He supports the latter with rational arguments: language requires the result after the condition, and reason rejects the effect preceding the cause. The commentary then addresses objections from grammarians and logicians, citing Al-Radi and others, arguing that a condition does not necessarily imply a cause; it only requires the result to be a consequence of the condition. The passage concludes with further discussion from Fath al-Qadir and Al-Nahr, refining the positions and responding to critiques. Throughout, the author maintains the Hanafi principle that conditional divorces are valid and must be given effect when possible, even if they lead to unusual consequences.

Key Terms
قبل (qabl): A temporal term meaning 'before' in a broad sense; used in conditional divorce to indicate precedence.
قبيل (qabil): A temporal term meaning 'just before' or 'shortly before', requiring a closely connected subsequent event to determine the moment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling if a husband says 'If I marry Zaynab just before (qabil) Amrah' and he marries Zaynab alone?
A) Zaynab is divorced immediately.
B) Zaynab is not divorced because the subsequent event (marrying Amrah) is required.
C) Zaynab is divorced only if he marries Amrah within one year.
D) The statement is void because 'qabil' is undefined.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi conditional divorce, 'qabl' gives immediate effect, while 'qabil' suspends the divorce until a closely connected subsequent event occurs.

Talaq — Divorce
advanced
12 min
The Circular Divorce (Al-Dawr): Can a Condition Precede its Result?
Ruling on circular conditional divorce (al-dawr)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi ruling on the circular divorce (al-dawr) and apply the majority view that the executed divorce does not occur.

Core Idea
In al-dawr, the condition cannot logically precede its result; the divorce is invalid.

A unique paradoxical case known as al-dawr (the circle) arises when a husband says, 'If I divorce you, you are divorced before that three times.' This creates a temporal loop: the suspended divorces would occur before the executed one, making the executed divorce impossible. Hanafi jurists are divided: some say all three occur, but the majority (mufta bihi) rule that the executed divorce does not occur because the effect cannot precede the cause. This lesson examines the rational, linguistic, and Shari'a-based proofs for the majority view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the paradox
Understand the statement: 'If I divorce you, you are divorced before that three times.' The condition is the husband's divorce; the result is three divorces occurring before that condition. This is a temporal contradiction.
This is called al-dawr (circular dependency).
2
State the majority Hanafi view
The majority of later Hanafi scholars hold that the executed divorce (the 'if' part) does not occur because the three suspended divorces would have to occur first, making the condition impossible.
3
Analyze the proof
The author uses rational, linguistic, and Shari'a proofs: (1) Reason: the effect cannot precede the cause; (2) Language: the result (jaza') comes after the condition; (3) Shari'a: the texts affirm divorce, but this would lead to its abrogation.
Argument type: dalil aqli.
4
Address objections
Consider the objection from grammarians like Al-Radi that a condition does not necessarily imply a cause. The Hanafi response is that even if not a cause, the result cannot logically precede the condition; it is a matter of dependency (tawaqquf).
The author engages with al-Radi's critique.
5
Derive the practical ruling
The mufta bihi position: if the husband then divorces her once, the wife is not divorced at all; if he divorces her twice, two divorces occur from the executed part but the suspended ones are invalid; if he divorces her three, all three executed divorces occur but the suspended ones are nonsense.
The suspended divorces are considered laghw (nonsense) because they cannot apply.

Book of Divorce, Chapter of Divorce. (Page 473) Nonsense: If he married her before yesterday, it occurs now. 'You are divorced as long as I have not divorced you' or 'whenever I do not divorce' or 'whenever I do not divorce you.' Al-Jami': It is not said that his statement 'before I marry you' is nonsense and that there is a separation between the condition and the conditioned, so that divorce should not be attached to marriage. For we say: We do not concede that it is nonsense; rather it is a clarification of what the beginning of the speech implied, because it necessitates that it is an immediate pronouncement by inserting the existence of his statement described as being before marriage. So it becomes as if he said to his wife: 'You are divorced this hour if you enter the house,' or 'You are divorced before you enter the house if you enter the house,' because his statement 'this hour' and 'before you enter' clarifies what the beginning of the speech required, although if he had made a separation there, it would be immediate. Here, if he made 'before I marry you' a separation, it would be nonsense, so it is more appropriate to consider it non-separating to validate the speech of a sane person. In al-Muhit: If he said, 'If I marry so-and-so after so-and-so, they are both divorced,' and he married them as stated, they are divorced because he attached divorce to marrying them. Because his statement 'after so-and-so' means after marrying so-and-so, so marrying so-and-so becomes mentioned necessarily, and he married them as conditioned, so the condition is found and divorce occurs. If he said, 'If I marry so-and-so before so-and-so, they are both divorced,' and he married the first, she is divorced because the condition regarding her is found, which is precedence, because describing something with precedence does not require the existence of what comes after. If he marries the second, she is also divorced. It is said: She should not be divorced. If he said, 'If I marry Zaynab before Amrah by a month, they are both divorced,' and he married Zaynab, then Amrah after her by a month, Zaynab is divorced immediately due to the existence of the condition, and it is not retroactive, as if he said, 'You are divorced before the arrival of so-and-so by a month.' Amrah is not divorced because he attached Amrah's divorce to a month before her marriage. If he said, 'If I marry Zaynab just before Amrah,' and he married Zaynab alone, she is not divorced because 'just before' is an expression for a subtle moment that connects to what follows it, and that is not known except by marrying Amrah, as if he said, 'You are divorced just before nightfall,' she is not divorced except at sunset. If he said 'before nightfall,' she is divorced immediately. If he marries Amrah after that, Zaynab is divorced, not Amrah. If the interval between the two marriages is long, neither is divorced. His statement: (If he married her before yesterday, it occurs now) because he attributed it to an incompatible state, and it cannot be validated as a report either, so it is an enactment, and enactment in the past is enactment in the present, so it occurs immediately. On this point, some later scholars among our masters ruled in the issue of al-dawr transmitted from later Shafi'is that it occurs, which is: If I divorce you, you are divorced before that three times. Most of them ruled that she is not divorced by executing her divorce because if it were executed, the suspended three would have occurred before it. The occurrence of three prior to the execution prevents the executed, because of the occurrence of the executed and the suspended. For enactment in the past is enactment in the present. We also say: This changes the ruling of language because the parts descend after the condition or with it, not before it, and also the ruling of reason because the object of the conditional particle is a cause and the result is an effect, and it is not reasonable for the effect to precede the cause. So his statement 'before it' is entirely nonsense; thus the divorce remains as the result of his statement 'you are divorced tomorrow' and after it with 'and', and in 'or' after it with 'or', it occurs after tomorrow. His statement: (If he said, 'If I marry Zaynab just before Amrah...') See what comes later from al-Tatimma about 'I am divorced from you is nonsense.' (Page 474) For the condition is not restricted by precedence, and by the ruling of the Shari'a because the texts speak of the legitimacy of divorce, and this leads to its abrogation. So from the mentioned issue, three occur: one executed and two from the suspended. If he divorced her two, they occur and one from the suspended; or if he divorced her three, they all occur, so the suspended divorce does not encounter eligibility and becomes nonsense. If he had said, 'If I divorce you, you are divorced before that,' then he divorced her once, two occur, the executed and the suspended, and analogize accordingly. Thus in Fath al-Qadir, but there is a critique because it is contradicted by His statement: 'And whatever blessing you have is from Allah' (16:53), for the first is the existence of blessing among the addressed, and the second is its being from Allah, and the first is not a cause for the second; rather the first is a branch of the second. Al-Radi said: It is not necessary with 'fa' that the first be a cause for the second; rather it is necessary that what is after 'fa' is a necessary consequence of the content of what precedes it, as in all forms of condition and result. So in His statement 'And whatever blessing you have is from Allah,' the blessing being from Him is a necessary consequence of its existence in meaning. Do not be deceived by the statement of some that the condition is a cause for the result. Its completion is in Sharh al-Mughni by al-Damamini from the discussion of 'ma' from the first discussion. Then his statement 'before it' is not nonsense due to lack of incompatibility, and it does not harm the legitimacy of divorce for one who chose that for himself and bound himself with it, as if he said, 'Every time I marry a woman, she is divorced,' which is valid with us even if it closes the door of legitimate marriage. In al-Qunyah from the end of the book of oaths: He said to her, 'Whenever my divorce falls upon you, you are before that divorced three times,' then he divorces her after... The grammarians clarify. His statement (by occurrence) means the occurrence of three, as required by the subdivision, and the declaration of that will also come in his speech. We will mention from Ibn Hajar the difference in the occurrence of the executed alone and the occurrence of three. His statement (because enactment in the past is enactment in the present) is apparently a justification for the first opinion of occurrence. His statement 'We also say...' supports it, so he delayed the justification of the first opinion until after the two opinions to connect the speech. His statement 'There is a critique because it is contradicted...' refutes his statement 'and by the ruling of reason'. His statement after that 'And it does not harm the legitimacy of divorce...' refutes his statement 'and by the ruling of the Shari'a'. In al-Nahr after mentioning the outcome of the author's speech: 'There is a critique from two aspects: first, what al-Radi said is only a madhhab; that in al-Mutawwal we do not concede that the grammatical condition is what the existence of something depends on; rather it is what is mentioned after 'in' and its sisters, upon which the occurrence of the content of the result is suspended, i.e., it is ruled that the content of that sentence occurs when it occurs. So it is mostly a necessary condition and the result is necessary, and the negation of the necessary necessitates the negation of the necessary condition without the converse. Then he said: The condition with them is more general than being a cause, like 'if the sun is shining, the world is illuminated,' or a condition like 'if I had money, I would perform Hajj,' or other like 'if the day existed, the sun would be shining.' Second, even if we concede that the conditional particle does not necessarily imply a cause, the invalidity of something preceding its condition is necessary because it depends on it, so it does not occur before it, as in al-Talwih. In it, the truth is that the invalidity of something preceding its condition is more apparent than the invalidity of its preceding the cause, because it is possible to be established by various causes. By this, his statement 'so it is not nonsense' is invalidated, i.e., his statement 'before it' due to lack of incompatibility. I say: It is not hidden from you that the first of these two aspects supports the author's statement in his claim that it is not necessary for the object of the conditional particle to be a cause and the result to be an effect, because it is clear that what is meant here by condition...

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, addressing rulings of conditional divorce involving temporal clauses like 'before' and 'after'. The author first discusses the case where a man says to his wife, 'If I married you before yesterday, you are divorced now.' He explains that such a statement is valid as an immediate divorce because it is incompatible with a past time reference; it cannot be a report, so it becomes an enactment that takes effect immediately. The commentary from Al-Jami' adds that saying 'before I marry you' is not nonsense; rather it clarifies that the divorce is immediate, similar to saying 'you are divorced this hour if you enter the house.' The passage then presents various scenarios from Al-Muhit: conditional divorces involving two women with 'after' and 'before' clauses, and the distinction between 'qabl' (before) and 'qabil' (just before). The core legal issue is how to interpret temporal conditions in divorce pronouncements. The author then transitions to a famous problematic case known as 'al-dawr' (the circle), where a husband says, 'If I divorce you, you are divorced before that three times.' This creates a paradox: the suspended divorce cannot precede the executed one. The author presents the view of some later Hanafi scholars that all three divorces occur, while most rule that the executed divorce does not occur because the three suspended divorces would have already occurred, making the executed one impossible. He supports the latter with rational arguments: language requires the result after the condition, and reason rejects the effect preceding the cause. The commentary then addresses objections from grammarians and logicians, citing Al-Radi and others, arguing that a condition does not necessarily imply a cause; it only requires the result to be a consequence of the condition. The passage concludes with further discussion from Fath al-Qadir and Al-Nahr, refining the positions and responding to critiques. Throughout, the author maintains the Hanafi principle that conditional divorces are valid and must be given effect when possible, even if they lead to unusual consequences.

Key Terms
قبل (qabl): Temporal term 'before'; in al-dawr, it indicates the suspended divorces precede the condition.
الدور (al-dawr): The circular conditional divorce where the condition and result are temporally interdependent.
إنشاء (insha'): Enactment; the minority view treats the statement as an immediate insha' of triple divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband says, 'If I divorce you, you are divorced before that three times.' He then divorces her once. What is the majority Hanafi ruling?
A) She is divorced three times immediately.
B) She is divorced once (the executed divorce) and the suspended divorces are ignored.
C) She is not divorced at all because the condition is impossible.
D) She is divorced four times: the executed one and the three suspended.

Key Takeaway
In the circular divorce (al-dawr), the majority Hanafi position is that the executed divorce is invalid because the effect cannot precede the cause, preserving the logical integrity of conditional pronouncements.

Talaq — Divorce
advanced
12 min
Hanafi View on Suspended Divorce with Temporal Paradox (Talaq al-Dawr)
Inter-madhhab dispute on conditional divorce with temporal circularity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a conditional divorce using 'qablah' and explain the Hanafi response to the charge of logical circularity.

Core Idea
A condition like 'if I divorce you, you are divorced before it' is valid in Hanafi law despite seeming circularity.

This lesson explores the Hanafi position on a peculiar divorce formulation: the husband says 'If I divorce you (once), you are divorced three times before it.' Logically, this seems impossible—how can a divorce occur before the condition that triggers it? Yet the Hanafi school holds the triple divorce valid, distinguishing between grammatical condition (shart nahwi) and legal cause (sabab). We'll analyze the debate with the Shafi'i school and the basis for the Hanafi view, which relies on the principle that the particle 'fa' does not necessitate causality, as shown in the Quranic verse {And whatever blessing you have is from Allah}.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Formulation and Paradox
Recognize the husband's statement: 'If I divorce you, then you are divorced three times before it.' The word 'qablah' (before it) creates a temporal circularity because the divorce (result) must occur before the condition (the divorce that triggers it).
2
Understand the Hanafi Linguistic Argument
The Hanafis argue that the grammatical condition (shart) does not have to be a cause (sabab). They cite Quran 16:53 ('Whatever blessing you have is from Allah') where the 'fa' indicates consequence, not causality. Thus, the condition is valid as a self-imposed obligation.
Al-Radi's explanation: 'fa' implies necessary consequence, not causal relation.
3
Apply the Ruling to the Example
When the husband subsequently gives one immediate talaq, the condition is triggered. According to the wording, three divorces fall: one immediate and two from the suspended condition. The condition is not void because it does not conflict with the legality of divorce.
Contrast with the case of 'every woman I marry is divorced' which blocks marriage but is still valid.
4
Examine the Shafi'i Objection
Al-Ghazali and others argue that such a condition closes the door of divorce (insidad bab al-talaq) due to impossibility. The most correct Shafi'i view holds that only the immediate divorce falls, not the suspended one.
The Shafi'i position is based on logical circularity making the condition invalid.
5
Note the Thamarah al-Khilaf
Hanafis enforce three divorces (irrevocable separation); Shafi'is enforce only one divorce (revocable if after first or second). This practical difference affects the marital status and the possibility of reconciliation.
Other schools (Maliki, Hanbali) also generally reject circularity but differ on the outcome.

إذا لم يتأكد، وإذا لم يقر شرعاً حرم التقليد فيه لأن التقليد في غير شرع هلاك، وهذه المسألة مخالفة للقواعد الشرعية فلا يصح التقليد فيها . قال القرافي : وهذا بيان حسن ظاهر. وقال الإمام ابن الصلاح ابن بانسداد باب الطلاق يشبه
Book of Divorce, Chapter of Divorce. The condition is not restricted to precedence [of the condition over the conditioned], and [it is also] for the ruling of the law because the texts speak of the legality of divorce, and this leads to raising [that legality], so it follows in the mentioned case the occurrence of three divorces: one immediate and two from the suspended. And if he divorces her twice, both fall and one from the suspended; or if he divorces her three times, all fall. Then the suspended divorce does not find legal capacity (ahliyyah) so it becomes void. And if he had said: 'If I divorce you, then you are divorced before it' then he divorces her once, two fall: the immediate and the suspended. And analogize to that. This is in Fath al-Qadir, but there is a problem: it is contradicted by His statement {And whatever blessing you have is from Allah} (An-Nahl 53), for the first is the existence of the blessing among the addressees, and the second is its being from Allah, and the first is not a cause for the second; rather the first is a branch of the second. And al-Radi said: It is not necessary with the particle 'fa' that the first be a cause for the second; rather what is necessary is that what comes after the 'fa' is a necessary consequence of the content of what precedes it, as in all forms of condition and result. So in His statement {And whatever blessing you have, it is from Allah}, the blessing being from Him is a necessary consequence of its existence in meaning. And do not be deceived by the saying of some that the condition is a cause for the result. End quote. And its completion is in Sharh al-Mughni by al-Damamini from the discussion of the first topic. Therefore, his saying 'before it' is not void due to lack of contradiction, and it does not harm the lifting of the legality of divorce upon one who chooses that for himself and binds himself to it, just as if he says 'Every time I marry a woman, she is divorced,' which is valid according to us even though it blocks the door of permissible marriage. And in al-Qunyah at the end of the Book of Oaths: He said to her, 'Whenever my divorce befalls you, then you are divorced before it three times,' then he later divorced her three times, all fall. And this is divorce of circularity (talaq al-dawr), and it does not fall according to al-Shafi'i. Al-Ghazali said in his Wajiz: If he says, 'If I divorce you, then you are divorced before it three times,' it closes the door of divorce according to the more apparent of the two views. And it was said: If he initiates one, that one falls; and it was said: three fall if it is after consummation. Then al-Ghazali said: If [he says], 'If you engage in permissible intercourse, then you are divorced before it,' and he [actually] does so, then there is no difference that she is not divorced. End quote. And the most correct according to the Shafi'is is what the two Shaykhs corrected: the occurrence of the immediate [divorce] without the suspended, as in Sharh al-Tanbih. And in it: If he said to his wife, 'Whenever you enter the house and you are my wife, then my slave is free before it, and whenever he enters while he is my slave, then you are divorced before it three times,' and they both enter together, the slave is not freed and the wife is not divorced due to the necessity of circularity, because if both were to occur, they would occur together before their entering, and if that were so, the slave would not be his slave at the time of entry, nor the woman his wife at that time, so the attribute upon which [the condition] is suspended would not be realized. And in this statement, the invalidity of circularity does not hold because it does not involve blocking the door of disposition. And if they enter in sequence, the suspen[sic]d divorce falls upon the later [in occurrence] but not the earlier. So if the woman enters first and then the slave, he is freed and she is not divorced, because at the time he entered he was not his slave, so the attribute for her divorce was not realized. And if he enters first, then she enters... The condition [here] is the grammatical condition, not legal. He said: Al-Ghazali said in his Wajiz, etc. I say: I have seen an independent treatise on this issue by the scholar Ibn Hajar al-Makki al-Shafi'i, and he narrated that al-Ghazali retracted at the end of his life from what he mentioned in his Wasit and Wajiz, and he said: Returning to the truth is better than persisting in falsehood. And he also narrated from Taj al-Subki that his father Taqi al-Subki retracted from the ruling on the Suraji issue and authored a treatise on it called 'al-Nur fi al-Dawr'. Then he quoted a group of Shafi'is that they authored treatises in which they refuted those among them who held the validity of circularity. He also said: And the majority of scholars from other schools besides our school [i.e., Shafi'i] are of the opinion that circularity is invalid. He said: This is indisputable. How can it be, when a group of Malikis, Hanafis, and Hanbalis have denounced those who claim the validity of circularity. And some imams have narrated from Abu Hanifa and his companions agreement on the invalidity of circularity, but what is transmitted from them is only about the occurrence of three or the immediate alone. And regarding the Hanbalis, there is no text from Ahmad on this issue. Al-Qadi said: She is divorced three times. Ibn 'Aqil said: She is divorced by the immediate only. End quote. Then he quoted twenty Shafi'i imams who agreed on the invalidity of circularity although they differed on the number that actually occurs. He also said: And 'Izz ibn 'Abd al-Salam vehemently criticized those who claim its validity, and suffice it to say that he is great, and therefore he was titled Sultan al-'Ulama'. His words as reported by his student Imam al-Qarafi: In this issue, there is no taqlid, and taqlid in it is sinful because the principle is that the judgment of a judge is overturned if it contradicts one of four things: consensus, text, principles, or clear analogy. And what is not legally recognized, if strengthened by a judge's ruling, is overturned, so it is more deserving if not strengthened. And if it is not legally recognized, taqlid in it is prohibited because taqlid in non-Shari'ah is destruction. And this issue contradicts legal principles, so taqlid in it is not valid. Al-Qarafi said: This is a good and clear explanation. And Imam Ibn al-Salah: Ibn Suraij is innocent of what is attributed to him, and the view of the groups of school followers and the majority of our companions is that the door of divorce is not blocked; rather divorce occurs, with differences on the quantity that occurs. And al-Zarkashi in al-Khadim said: And al-Saruji from the Hanafis went to extremes and said: The saying that the door of divorce is blocked resembles...
Source pages p. 474

Commentary
This passage is an intra-madhhab and inter-madhhab dispute regarding the validity of a suspended divorce that contains a temporal paradox (the use of 'قبله' before the condition). The author, from the Hanafi perspective, first presents the ruling that if a husband says 'If I divorce you, you are divorced three times before it' and then actually divorces her with one pronouncement, all three fall: one immediate and two from the suspended condition. This is based on the principle that the condition is not restricted to precedence; rather, the legal effect takes place according to the wording. The author then criticizes this ruling by citing a Quranic verse (An-Nahl 53) as an example that the particle 'fa' does not necessarily indicate causality. He quotes al-Radi to support that a condition does not have to be a cause; it is merely a marker of a necessary consequence. Therefore, the word 'قبله' does not create an impossible circularity, and the divorce is not void. The author compares this to the case of saying 'Every woman I marry is divorced,' which is valid despite blocking the door of marriage. Then the text shifts to the Shafi'i position, quoting al-Ghazali that such a condition would close the door of divorce, with different views on what actually falls. The Shafi'i correct position is that only the immediate divorce falls, not the suspended. The passage then narrates a detailed scenario with a slave and wife to illustrate the logical circularity. The majority of scholars, including Hanafis, Malikis, and Hanbalis, consider such circularity invalid. The discussion ends with strong condemnations of taqlid in this issue, citing authorities like 'Izz ibn 'Abd al-Salam and al-Qarafi, emphasizing that the ruling contradicts established principles.

Key Terms
الطلاق المعلق (al-talaq al-mu'allaq): Divorce that is suspended on the occurrence of a future condition (e.g., 'if you enter the house, you are divorced').
الدور (al-dawr): Circularity; a logical paradox where the condition and conditioned depend on each other, e.g., divorce before the condition that triggers it.
الشرط النحوي (al-shart al-nahwi): Grammatical condition; a linguistic structure using 'if' that indicates dependency in meaning, not necessarily a legal cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view presented in the passage, if a husband says 'If I divorce you, you are divorced three times before it' and then gives one talaq (immediate divorce), what is the outcome?
A) No divorce occurs because the condition is impossible.
B) Only one talaq (the immediate one) falls.
C) Three talaq fall: one immediate and two from the suspended condition.
D) The marriage is annulled automatically.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school enforces a conditional divorce even when it involves temporal paradox, based on the principle that the condition is a linguistic dependency, not a legal cause.

Talaq — Divorce

Invalidity of Talaq al-Dawr (Circular Divorce)
Refutes the invalid concept of talaq al-dawr and declares it contrary to consensus

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why talaq al-dawr is invalid in the Hanafi school and identify the consensus that rejects it.

Core Idea
A circular divorce is impossible; it is void by consensus.

Divorce is a serious matter with strict conditions. Sometimes a husband tries to set up a conditional divorce that leads to a logical impossibility—a circular dependency. This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on such 'circular divorce' (talaq al-dawr) and shows why it is utterly void, based on the consensus of the scholars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? It is a suspended divorce where the divorce is made conditional on an event that itself depends on the divorce. The ruling is that the suspension is valid but the divorce is void (batil) due to impossibility.
Impossibility here is logical, not just practical.
2
Locate the dalil
The text cites consensus (ijma) of the ummah. The broader context mentions that the Companions and later imams agreed that divorce by a legally responsible person is valid, and talaq al-dawr contradicts that principle.
Ijma is a primary source of evidence.
3
Examine the wajh al-dalalah
How does consensus prove invalidity? Since the ummah has agreed on the conditions of valid divorce, any form that makes occurrence impossible falls outside that consensus. Circular divorce leads to a contradiction: the divorce would have to occur before its own cause.
This is a logical argument, not just textual.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
A minority among Shafi'is (e.g., Ibn Surayj) considered such a formulation valid. However, the majority of Shafi'is and all other schools reject it.
The text emphasizes that this is not a legitimate difference.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The Hanafi position is the mufta bihi: talaq al-dawr is invalid and does not occur. This rests on the strength of consensus and logical necessity.
No later tarjih is needed; the issue is settled.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفيه: لو قال إن ظاهرت منك أو آليت أو لاعنت أو فسخت النكاح بعيب فأنت طالق قبله ثلاثاً ثم وجد المعلق به صح ولغا تعليق الطلاق لاستحالة وقوعه ا هـ
And in it: If he said, 'If I perform zihar on you, or ila, or li'an, or annul the marriage due to a defect, then you are divorced before it three times,' then the suspended thing occurred, the suspension of divorce is valid but void because of the impossibility of its occurrence. End.
Source pages p. 476

Commentary
This brief passage from the commentary addresses a specific case: a husband suspends divorce on the occurrence of zihar, ila, li'an, or annulment, and further says 'you are divorced before it three times.' The ruling is that the suspension itself is valid (meaning the condition is properly set), but the suspended divorce is void (لغا) because it is impossible for the divorce to occur before its own cause. This is the classic formulation of talaq al-dawr (circular divorce), which is logically impossible. The commentator's note serves as a reminder that such formulations are invalid, and the earlier longer digression reinforces that this view is rejected by consensus and is not a legitimate difference of opinion.

Key Terms
طلاق الدور (talaq al-dawr): A circular divorce where the occurrence of divorce is made conditional on an event that itself is conditional on divorce, leading to impossibility.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is valid and divorce occurs.
B) It is valid but requires compensation.
C) It is invalid because it is impossible.
D) It is valid only if the husband intends it.

Key Takeaway
Talaq al-dawr is invalid in the Hanafi school because it creates a logical impossibility, and this ruling is confirmed by consensus of the scholars.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate

Ruling on Additive Ta'liq: 'You are divorced as long as I have not divorced you'
Explains the ruling when husband uses additive temporal phrases like 'ما لم أطلقك'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi ruling when a husband uses an additive temporal phrase like 'ما لم أطلقك' and explain why silence triggers immediate divorce.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, 'ما لم' in divorce is additive, not conditional—silence means the time of not divorcing is already past.

This lesson unpacks a key ruling in Hanafi talaq: when a husband utters phrases like 'You are divorced as long as I have not divorced you' and remains silent, the divorce takes effect immediately. The passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies that such constructions are additive (idafi), not suspending (ta'liqi), and provides linguistic proof from Quranic usage. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying divorce rulings in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the construction type
Determine whether the phrase is additive (idafi) or suspending (ta'liqi). The key is the particle 'ma' and 'lam'—Hanafi scholars agree they indicate time, not condition.
Note that 'ma' in 'ma lam' functions as a time adverb, similar to its use in the Quranic phrase 'ma dumtu hayyan'.
2
Analyze the linguistic proof
Examine the Quranic verse (Surah Maryam 19:31) 'ما دمت حيا' to see how 'ma' denotes duration. This supports the additive interpretation.
3
Distinguish between 'lam' and 'la'
Understand that 'lam' (jussive) turns the verb to past tense with negation, so the time of not divorcing is considered elapsed. In contrast, 'la' (negative) is for the future, delaying divorce until a customary period (e.g., six months for 'hin').
For phrases like 'zaman la utallik', divorce is not immediate; a period must pass.
4
Apply the ruling to other temporal particles
Extend the ruling to words like 'mata', 'hin', 'zaman', 'yawm', 'hayth'. For 'mata lam', the same logic applies. For 'hayth', it denotes place, so divorce occurs in a place where he did not divorce her.
If the phrase includes 'kullama' (every time), multiple divorces occur consecutively.

قوله : (أنت طالق ما لم أطلقك أو متى لم أطلقك أو متى ما لم أطلقك وسكت طلقت) بيان لما إذا أضاف إلى مطلق الوقت. وذكرهم «أن» و «إذا» هنا بالتبعية وإلا فالمناسب لهما التعليق لا الإضافة. وإنما طلقت بالسكوت لأن متى ظرف زمان وكذا «ما» تكون مصدرية عن ظرف الزمان كما في قوله تعالى ما دمت حيال [مريم : ٣١] أي مدة دوام حياتي أو مدة دوامي حياً. وهي وإن استعملت للشرط لكن اتفق العلماء على أنها هنا للوقت، ولذا نقل في فتح القدير اتفاق العلماء على وقوع الطلاق بالسكوت فصار حاصل المعنى إضافة طلاقها إلى زمان خال من طلاقها وهو حاصل بسكوته قيد بقوله «وسكت» لأنه لو قال نائبة مذاهب النصارى أنه لا يمكن الزوج إيقاع طلاق على زوجته مدة عمره. وقال الإمام الكمال ابن الرداد شارح الإرشاد المعتمد في الفتوى وقوع الطلاق المنجز وهو المنقول عن ابن سريج وصححه جمع وعليه العمل في الديار المصرية والشامية وهو القوى في الدليل وعزاه الرافعي إلى أبي حنيفة . هذا حاصل ما أردت تلخيصه من مؤلف ابن حجر، وتقدم عن المحقق ابن الهمام تقوية القول بالوقوع، ونقل الغزي في منح الغفار أول كتاب الطلاق رد القول بخلافه بأبلغ وجه حيث قال : وفي جواهر الفتاوى قال أبو العباس بن سريج من أصحاب الشافعي: إذا قال الرجل لامرأته إن طلقتك ثلاثاً فأنت طالق قبله ثلاثاً ثم أوقع الطلاق عليها لا يقع أبداً. وأنكر عليه . جميع أئمة المسلمين من أصحاب الشافعي أيضاً مثل إمام الحرمين والشيخ أبي اسحق والإمام الغزالي، وهذا قول مخترع مخالف لأهل القبلة فإن الأمة أجمعت من الصحابة والتابعين وأئمة السلف من أبي حنيفة والشافعي وأصحابهما على أن طلاق المكلف واقع وقد قال صلى الله عليه وسلم من خالف الجماعة قيد شبر فقد خلع ربقة الإسلام وعن بعض مشايخنا أنه رأى النبي في المنام فسأله عن طلاق الدور فقال صلى الله عليه وسلم «من قال بطلاق الدور فقد أضل أمتي فقال : لا يقبل مني . فقال ما عليك إلا البلاغ». ثم بحث في الاستدلال على بطلانه ثم قال : ولو حكم حاكم بصحة الدور وبقاء النكاح وعدم وقوع الطلاق لا ينفذ حكمه ويجب على حاكم آخر تفريقهما لأن مثل هذا لا يعد خلافاً لأنه قول مجهول باطل فاسد ظاهر البطلان . اهـ إلى هنا كلام المنح.

موصلاً أنت طالق بركما سيأتي . ومثل متى حين وزمان وحيث ويوم، فلو قال حين لم أطلقك ولا نية له فهي طالق حين سكت، وكذا زمان لم أطلقك وحيث لم أطلقك ويوم لم أطلقك إذا كان بـ«لم الجازمة فلو كان بلا النافية نحو زمان لا أطلقك أو حين لا أطلقك بحرف «لا» النافية لم تطلق حتى تمضي ستة أشهر. والفرق بين الحرفين أن «لم» تقلب المضارع ماضياً مع النفي وقد وجد زمان لم يطلقها فيه فوقع وكلمة (( لا ) للاستقبال غالباً فإن لم يكن له نية لا يقع في الحال. وإنما يراد بـ«حين ستة أشهر لأنه أوسط استعمالاته والأربعين سنة وستة أشهر في قوله تعالى فسبحان الله حين تمسون وحين تصبحون [الروم: ۱۷] هل أتى على الإنسان حين من الدهر ﴾ [الدهر : ١] تؤتي أكلها كل حين بإذن ربها [ ابراهيم : ٢٥] والزمان كالحين لأنهما سواء في الاستعمال. ولو قال أطلقك لم تطلق حتى يمضي يوم الكل من المحيط . وأما حيث فهي للمكان وكم مكان لم يطلقها فيه. كذا في فتح القدير فكأنه قال أنت طالق في مكان لم أطلقك فيه. وذكر في المغني أن الأخفش جعلها للزمان أيضاً فلا إشكال. وقيد بما ذكر لأنه لو قال كلما لم أطلقك فأنت طالق وسكت يقع الثلاث متتابعاً لا جملة لأنها تقتضي عموم الانفراد لا عموم الاجتماع، فإن لم تكن مدخولاً بها بانت بواحدة فقط . وقيد بمطلق الوقت لأنه لو قيده مع العدم كأن قال إن لم تدخلي الدار سنة فأنت طالق فمضت السنة قبل الدخول طلقت كما في الإيلاء. كذا في البدائع.
His saying: (You are divorced as long as I have not divorced you, or whenever I have not divorced you, or whenever I have not divorced you, and he remained silent, she is divorced) is an explanation of when he adds to absolute time. And their mention of 'an' and 'idha' here is incidental, otherwise what is appropriate for them is suspension not addition. She is divorced by silence because 'mata' is a time adverb, and similarly 'ma' is a masdariyyah in place of a time adverb, as in His saying 'ma dumtu hayyan' (as long as I am alive) [Maryam:31], i.e., the duration of my life or the duration of my being alive. And although it is used for condition, the scholars agreed that here it is for time. Hence it is transmitted in Fath al-Qadir the agreement of scholars on the occurrence of divorce by silence. So the meaning becomes adding her divorce to a time devoid of her divorce, which is achieved by his silence. He restricted it with his saying 'and he remained silent' because if he said ... the sect of the Christians that the husband cannot effect a divorce upon his wife for the duration of his life. And Imam Kamal ibn al-Raddad, commentator of al-Irshad, said: the relied-upon in fatwa is the occurrence of the immediate divorce, which is reported from Ibn Surayj, and a group authenticated it, and upon it is the practice in the Egyptian and Syrian lands, and it is strong in evidence, and al-Rafi'i attributed it to Abu Hanifah. This is the gist of what I wanted to summarize from the work of Ibn Hajar, and it preceded from al-Muhaqqiq Ibn al-Humam strengthening the view of occurrence. And al-Ghazzi transmitted in Minah al-Ghaffar at the beginning of Kitab al-Talaq a refutation of the opposing view in the most eloquent manner where he said: In Jawahir al-Fatawa, Abu al-Abbas ibn Surayj from the Shafi'is said: If a man says to his wife 'If I divorce you three times, then you are divorced before it three times,' then he effects divorce upon her, it never occurs. And all the imams of the Muslims from the Shafi'is as well, such as Imam al-Haramayn, Shaykh Abu Ishaq, and Imam al-Ghazali, rejected this. And this is an invented saying opposing the people of the qibla, for the ummah has unanimously agreed from the Companions, the Followers, and the early imams from Abu Hanifah and al-Shafi'i and their companions that the divorce of the legally responsible person occurs. And the Prophet (sallallahu alayhi wa sallam) said: 'Whoever opposes the community by a span has removed the noose of Islam from his neck.' And from some of our shaykhs that he saw the Prophet in a dream and asked him about talaq al-dawr, and he said: 'Whoever says with talaq al-dawr has led my ummah astray.' He said: 'It is not accepted from me.' He said: 'You only have to convey.' Then he discussed the evidence for its invalidity, then said: 'If a judge judges the validity of al-dawr and the continuation of the marriage and the non-occurrence of divorce, his judgment is not executed, and another judge must separate them, because such is not considered a difference of opinion, for it is an unknown false invalid opinion manifestly invalid.' End of the words from al-Manh. (1) (1) Reported by Abu Dawud in Kitab al-Sunnah, Bab 27; al-Tirmidhi in Kitab al-Adab, Bab 78; al-Nasa'i in al-Sariq, Bab 1; Ahmad in his Musnad (3/332, 4/130, 202). Kitab al-Talaq, Bab al-Talaq. One of them: You are divorced as long as I have not divorced you, you are divorced, this divorce occurred you are such. The day I marry you, from the hour, the day not, connected, you are divorced, as will come. And like mata: hin, zaman, hayth, and yawm. So if he says 'when I have not divorced you' and has no intention, she is divorced when he is silent. Similarly, 'zaman lam utallik', 'hayth lam utallik', and 'yawm lam utallik' if it is with the jussive 'lam'. But if it is with the negative 'la' like 'zaman la utallik' or 'hin la utallik' with the negative 'la', she is not divorced until six months pass. The difference between the two particles is that 'lam' changes the present tense to past with negation, and a time in which he did not divorce her has been found, so it occurs, while 'la' is for future mostly. If he has no intention, it does not occur immediately. 'Hin' is interpreted as six months because it is the most common usage, and forty years and six months appear in the verses: 'So exalt Allah when you reach the evening and when you become morning' [Rum:17], 'Has there come upon man a period of time' [Dahr:1], 'It yields its fruit every season' [Ibrahim:25]. And 'zaman' is like 'hin' because they are the same in usage. And if he says 'I divorce you' she is not divorced until a day passes. All from al-Muhit. As for 'hayth', it is for place, and how many a place he did not divorce her in. Thus in Fath al-Qadir, as if he said 'You are divorced in a place in which I have not divorced you.' And in al-Mughni it is mentioned that al-Akhfash made it for time as well, so no problem. He restricted with what was mentioned because if he said 'every time I have not divorced you, you are divorced' and he is silent, three divorces occur consecutively not simultaneously because they imply generality of individual instances not generality of collection. If she is not consummated, she becomes separated with one only. And he restricted with absolute time because if he restricted it with non-existence like saying 'If you do not enter the house for a year, you are divorced' and the year passes before entry, she is divorced as in ila. Thus in al-Bada'i.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explaining a ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq on additive ta'liq (conditional divorce). The ruling: when a husband says 'You are divorced as long as I have not divorced you' (or using 'mata' or 'mata ma' with negation) and remains silent thereafter, the divorce takes effect immediately. The author clarifies that this is an additive (idafi) construction, not a suspending (ta'liqi) one, because 'mata' and 'ma' here denote time, not condition. The proof is the Quranic usage 'ma dumtu hayyan' (as long as I am alive) from Surah Maryam. The scholars agree that 'ma' here indicates a time period. Thus the meaning is: I add your divorce to a time that is empty of divorce, which is achieved by his silence. If he had spoken, it would not be empty. The commentator also discusses the digression about talaq al-dawr (circular divorce) reported from some Shafi'is, which is categorically rejected by the consensus of the ummah as invalid and heretical. He then returns to the main topic, discussing other temporal words: 'hin', 'zaman', 'hayth', 'yawm'. The key distinction is between using 'lam' (jussive) and 'la' (negative). With 'lam', the action of not divorcing is considered a past event, so silence triggers divorce immediately. With 'la', it indicates future, so divorce is delayed until a period passes (estimated as six months for 'hin', based on customary usage). For 'hayth', it is primarily for place, so divorce occurs in a place where he did not divorce her. If he says 'kullama' (every time), multiple divorces occur consecutively for each instance of silence. The passage ends with a note on conditional ta'liq with a time limit, comparing it to ila.

Key Terms
ما لم (ma lam): A phrase meaning 'as long as not' used for additive time, not conditional suspension.
لم الجازمة (lam al-jazimah): The jussive particle 'lam' that negates the past and gives a past meaning to the present tense verb.
لا النافية (la al-nafiyyah): The negative particle 'la' that primarily negates the future.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says to his wife 'You are divorced as long as I have not divorced you' (أنت طالق ما لم أطلقك) and then remains silent, what is the ruling according to the Hanafi school?
A) The divorce does not occur until he dies or she dies.
B) The divorce occurs immediately upon his silence.
C) The divorce occurs only if he explicitly intends divorce.
D) The divorce occurs after six months of silence.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes additive (idafi) from suspending (ta'liqi) constructions in divorce; phrases like 'ما لم أطلقك' indicate additive time, so silence triggers immediate divorce.

Conditional Divorce with 'In Lam' Requires Death
Explains the ruling for conditional 'in lam' requiring death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for divorce suspended on 'in lam utallik' and explain the moment of occurrence (just before death) and its inheritance implications.

Core Idea
Divorce with 'in lam utallik' occurs only at the last moment of life because the condition can only be verified then.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq by Ibn Nujaym clarifies a subtle point in conditional divorce. Unlike additive phrases (e.g., 'ma lam utallik' which triggers immediately), the phrase 'in lam utallik' (if I have not divorced you) suspends divorce on the negation of divorce. This negation can only be certain at death — so the divorce occurs just before the first to die, with special rules to prevent the husband from cheating the wife out of inheritance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional phrase
The husband says 'in lam utallik' (if I have not divorced you) or similar. Recognize that this is a suspensive condition (ta'liq) not an additive phrase. The condition is that he does not divorce her at all during his life.
Contrast with additive 'ma lam utallik' where the condition is already fulfilled.
2
Explain why death is required
The condition 'he does not divorce her' can only be verified when it is impossible for him to divorce her. That only happens at the moment of death (al-ya's 'an al-hayah). As long as both are alive, he could still divorce her, so the condition is not yet certain.
The Hanafis say divorce occurs in the last indivisible part of life (akhir juz' la yatajazza).
3
Determine the moment and inheritance effect
Divorce occurs just before the death of the first to die. If the husband dies first, the wife is divorced just before his death. If she was consummated (madhul biha), she inherits from him by the rule of furar (to prevent cheating). If she dies first, divorce occurs just before her death, and the husband does not inherit because the marriage ends before death.
The text corrects a misinterpretation: the wife inherits regardless of whether the divorce was three or one, as long as she was consummated.
4
Compare with the 'entry' analogy
The author contrasts with the case 'If I do not enter the house, you are divorced.' Here, if the wife dies first, the husband could still enter after her death, so death does not verify the condition; divorce does not occur. But with divorce, the wife's death ends the legal capacity for divorce, so despair is realized.
This shows the unique nature of the suspensive condition in divorce.
5
Synthesize the corrected ruling
The commentator notes that some incorrectly restrict the inheritance rule to non-consummated or three divorces. The correct Hanafi position is that inheritance exclusion is absolute: if the wife is divorced just before her death, the husband never inherits from her, regardless of consummation or number of divorces.
The author equates 'in' and 'idha' — both are treated the same.

قوله : (وفي إن لم أطلقك أو إذا لم أطلقك أو إذا ما لم أطلقك لا حتى يموت أحدهما) أي لا يقع الطلاق إلا بموت أحدهما قبل التطليق عند عدم النية ودلالة الفور لأن الشرط أن لا يطلقها وذلك لا يتحقق إلا باليأس عن الحياة وهو في آخر جزء من أجزاء الحياة. أما في موته فظاهر ولم يقدره المتقدمون بل قالوا : تطلق قبيل موته، فإن كانت مذخولاً بها ورثته بحكم الفرار وإن كان الطلاق ثلاثاً وإلا لا ترثه. وأشار بقوله «بموت أحدهما» أن موتها كموته. وصححه في الهداية، ولا يرد عليه ما لو قال إن لم أدخل الدار فأنت طالق حيث يقع بموته لا بموتها لأنه يمكنه الدخول بعد موتها فلا يتحقق اليأس بموتها فلا يقع الطلاق، أما الطلاق فإنه يتحقق اليأس عنه بموتها لعدم المحلية، وإذا حكمنا بوقوعه قبيل موتها لا يرث منها الزوج لأنها بانت قبيل الموت فلم يبق بينهما زوجية حال الموت. إنما حكمنا بالبينونة وإن كان المعلق صريحاً لانتفاء العدة كغير المدخول بها لأن الفرض أن الوقوع في آخر جزء لا يتجزى فلم يله إلا الموت وبه تبين ولذا جعل المصنف الوقوع بالموت وإن كان قبيله وقد ظهر أن عدم إرثه منها ،مطلق سواء كانت مدخولاً بها أو لا، ثلاثاً أو واحدة، وبه تبين أن تقييد الشارح عدمه بعدم الدخول أو الثلاث غير صحيح، وتسوية المصنف بين «إن» و
His saying: (And regarding 'in lam utallik', 'idha lam utallik', or 'idha ma lam utallik', not until one of them dies) i.e., divorce does not occur except with the death of one of them before [actual] divorce, in the absence of intention and indication of immediacy, because the condition is that he does not divorce her, and that is not realized except with despair of life, which is in the last indivisible part of life. As for his death, it is apparent, and the early scholars did not estimate it; rather they said: She is divorced just before his death. If she is one with whom consummation occurred, she inherits from him by the ruling of furar (flight from inheritance), even if the divorce is three; otherwise she does not inherit from him. And he indicated by his saying 'by the death of one of them' that her death is like his death. And it is authenticated in al-Hidayah. And it is not objected upon by the case where he said 'If I do not enter the house, you are divorced' where it occurs by his death not by her death, because he can enter after her death, so despair is not realized by her death, so divorce does not occur. As for divorce, despair is realized by her death due to lack of [her] legal capacity. And if we rule that it occurs just before her death, the husband does not inherit from her because she became separated just before death, so there is no marriage at the moment of death. We only rule separation even if the suspended [divorce] was explicit, because the waiting period is absent like a non-consummated woman, because the assumption is that the occurrence is in the last indivisible part, after which only death follows, and thus separation occurs. Hence the author made the occurrence at death even though it is before it. And it has become apparent that the omission of inheritance from her is absolute, whether she is consummated or not, three or one. And thus it is apparent that the commentator's restriction of its omission to non-consummation or three is incorrect, and the author's equating between 'in' and...
Source pages p. 477

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling for conditional phrases like 'in lam utallik' (if I have not divorced you) or 'idha lam utallik' (when I have not divorced you). Unlike the additive phrases discussed earlier, these are truly conditional (ta'liqi). The condition is that he does not divorce her at all. This condition can only be verified with certainty at the moment of death, because as long as both are alive, he could still divorce her in the future. Therefore, divorce occurs just before death (in the last indivisible moment of life). The text clarifies that the same applies if the wife dies first, because legal capacity for divorce ends. However, if the husband dies first, divorce occurs just before his death. If the wife was consummated, she inherits from him (to prevent the husband from using this to deprive her of inheritance, called furar). If not consummated, she does not inherit, because she already became separated before his death. The author emphasizes that the loss of inheritance right is absolute regardless of consummation or number of divorces, correcting a misinterpretation by some commentators. The excerpt ends with a comparison between 'in' and other particles.

Key Terms
إن لم (in lam): A conditional phrase 'if not' that suspends divorce on a condition that can only be fulfilled at death.
الفرار (al-furar): The husband's attempt to deprive the wife of inheritance by divorcing her near death.
اليأس عن الحياة (al-ya's 'an al-hayah): Despair of life, i.e., the moment of death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband says, 'If I have not divorced you by my death, you are divorced.' He dies without divorcing her. According to the Hanafi school as explained in the text, when does the divorce occur?
A) At the moment he said it.
B) Never, because he died before divorcing.
C) Just before his death, in the last indivisible moment.
D) After his death, when the condition is verified.

Key Takeaway
Divorce with 'in lam utallik' occurs only at the death of one spouse, not earlier, because only then is it certain that no divorce occurred during life; this prevents abuse of inheritance.

The Effect of 'Lam' vs 'La' in Conditional Divorce
Explains rulings for conditional divorce using 'lam', 'la', and temporal particles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the legal effects of the particles 'lam' (past) and 'la' (future) in conditional divorce, and apply the default time period for the word 'hin'.

Core Idea
Past negation ('lam') triggers immediate divorce; future negation ('la') requires six months; dying is the ultimate despair for 'in lam utallik'.

The words we choose to express a condition can change the entire ruling. In divorce law, a slight shift from 'lam' to 'la' alters when, or if, the divorce takes effect. This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq unpacks these linguistic nuances, showing how a single letter can determine legal outcomes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the particle
Determine whether the conditional uses 'lam' (jussive, negating the past) or 'la' (future negation). This is the pivotal linguistic distinction.
'Lam' refers to a past time when he did not divorce; 'la' looks to the future.
2
Apply the time frame
For 'lam', the divorce occurs immediately upon utterance (or silence) because a past moment of not divorcing exists. For 'la', the divorce waits; the default period for temporal words like 'hin' is six months.
The six-month default is derived from the common usage of 'hin' in the Quran.
3
Handle 'in lam utallik'
If the condition is 'if I have not divorced you' (in lam utallik), divorce does not occur until one spouse dies, because only then is it certain he will never divorce her.
The divorce is considered to occur just before death, affecting inheritance.
4
Consider contextual indications
Even with 'la', if there is a contextual indication of immediacy (like the wife's demand), the divorce may occur immediately rather than after six months.
This is discussed in the second lesson on fawr.
