أحدهما أنت طالق ما لم أطلقك أنت طالق طلقت هذه الطلقة أنت كذا: يوم أتزوجك من الساعة يوم لا موصلاً أنت طالق بركما سيأتي. ومثل متى حين وزمان وحيث ويوم، فلو قال حين لم أطلقك ولا نية له فهي طالق حين سكت، وكذا زمان لم أطلقك وحيث لم أطلقك ويوم لم أطلقك إذا كان بـ«لم الجازمة فلو كان بلا النافية نحو زمان لا أطلقك أو حين لا أطلقك بحرف «لا» النافية لم تطلق حتى تمضي ستة أشهر. والفرق بين الحرفين أن «لم» تقلب المضارع ماضياً مع النفي وقد وجد زمان لم يطلقها فيه فوقع وكلمة (( لا ) للاستقبال غالباً فإن لم يكن له نية لا يقع في الحال. وإنما يراد بـ«حين ستة أشهر لأنه أوسط استعمالاته والأربعين سنة وستة أشهر في قوله تعالى فسبحان الله حين تمسون وحين تصبحون [الروم: ۱۷] هل أتى على الإنسان حين من الدهر ﴾ [الدهر : ١] تؤتي أكلها كل حين بإذن ربها [ ابراهيم : ٢٥] والزمان كالحين لأنهما سواء في الاستعمال. ولو قال أطلقك لم تطلق حتى يمضي يوم الكل من المحيط . وأما حيث فهي للمكان وكم مكان لم يطلقها فيه. كذا في فتح القدير فكأنه قال أنت طالق في مكان لم أطلقك فيه. وذكر في المغني أن الأخفش جعلها للزمان أيضاً فلا إشكال. وقيد بما ذكر لأنه لو قال كلما لم أطلقك فأنت طالق وسكت يقع الثلاث متتابعاً لا جملة لأنها تقتضي عموم الانفراد لا عموم الاجتماع، فإن لم تكن مدخولاً بها بانت بواحدة فقط . وقيد بمطلق الوقت لأنه لو قيده مع العدم كأن قال إن لم تدخلي الدار سنة فأنت طالق فمضت السنة قبل الدخول طلقت كما في الإيلاء. كذا في البدائع. قوله : (وفي إن لم أطلقك أو إذا لم أطلقك أو إذا ما لم أطلقك لا حتى يموت أحدهما) أي لا يقع الطلاق إلا بموت أحدهما قبل التطليق عند عدم النية ودلالة الفور لأن الشرط أن لا يطلقها وذلك لا يتحقق إلا باليأس عن الحياة وهو في آخر جزء من أجزاء الحياة. أما في موته فظاهر ولم يقدره المتقدمون بل قالوا : تطلق قبيل موته، فإن كانت مذخولاً بها ورثته بحكم الفرار وإن كان الطلاق ثلاثاً وإلا لا ترثه. وأشار بقوله «بموت أحدهما» أن موتها كموته. وصححه في الهداية، ولا يرد عليه ما لو قال إن لم أدخل الدار فأنت طالق حيث يقع بموته لا بموتها لأنه يمكنه الدخول بعد موتها فلا يتحقق اليأس بموتها فلا يقع الطلاق، أما الطلاق فإنه يتحقق اليأس عنه بموتها لعدم المحلية، وإذا حكمنا بوقوعه قبيل موتها لا يرث منها الزوج لأنها بانت قبيل الموت فلم يبق بينهما زوجية حال الموت. إنما حكمنا بالبينونة وإن كان المعلق صريحاً لانتفاء العدة كغير المدخول بها لأن الفرض أن الوقوع في آخر جزء لا يتجزى فلم يله إلا الموت وبه تبين ولذا جعل المصنف الوقوع بالموت وإن كان قبيله وقد ظهر أن عدم إرثه منها مطلق سواء كانت مدخولاً بها أو لا، ثلاثاً أو واحدة، وبه تبين أن تقييد الشارح عدمه بعدم الدخول أو الثلاث غير صحيح، وتسوية المصنف بين «إن» و «إذا» مذهب أبي حنيفة فهي عنده إذا جوزي بها حرف لمجرد الشرط لأن مجرده ربط خاص وهو من معاني الحروف، وقد تكون الكلمة حرفاً أو اسماً، فلما كانت للشرط والوقت لم يقع الطلاق للحال بالشك، وعندهما كمتى للوقت. وحاصله أن الإمام بنى مذهبه على إن «إذا» تخرج عن الظرفية وتكون لمحض الشرط وهو قول بعض النحاة كما ذكره في المغني لكن ذكر أن الجمهور على أنها للظرفية متضمنة معنى الشرطية وأنها لا تخرج عن الظرفية وهو مرجح لقولهما هنا، وقدر حجه في فتح القدير. ولا يرد على أبي حنيفة أنت طالق إذا شئت حيث وافقهما أنها كمتى فلا يخرج الأمر من يدها ولو كانت كـ«إن الخرج الأمر من يدها الشك الخروج بعد تحقق الدخول واعترض عليه بأن وقوع الشك في الشرطية والظرفية يوجب وقوعه في الحل والحرمة في الحال فكان ينبغي أن تحرم تقديماً للمحرم كما قالا. وأجيب بأن الشك لا شيئاً إنما ذلك يوجب تعارض دليل الحرمة مع دليل الحل فالاحتياط العمل بدليل الحرمة، أما هنا لو اعتبرنا الحرمة لم نعمل بدليل بل بالشك. وقيدنا بعدم النية لأنه لو نوى بـ«إذا» معنى متى» صدق اتفاقاً قضاء وديانة لتشديده على نفسه، وكذا إذا نوى بـ«إذا» معنى «إن» على قولهما. وينبغي أن يصدق عندهما ديانة فقط لأنها عندهما ظاهرة في الظرفية والشرطية احتمال فلا يصدقه القاضي. وقيدنا بعدم دلالة الفور لأنه لو قامت دلالة عليه عمل بها، ولذا قال في القنية : لو قالت له طلقني فقال إن لم أطلقك يقع على الفور. وقد زاد هذا القيد في المبتغى بالمعجمة فقال : لو قال لها إن لم تخبريني بكذا فأنت طالق فهو على الأبد إن لم يكن ثمة ما يدل على الفور ا.هـ. وتبعه عليه في فتح القدير وقال: إنه قيد حسن. ومن ثم قالوا: لو أراد أن يجامع امرأته فلم تطاوعه فقال إن لم تدخلي البيت معي فأنت طالق فدخلت بعد ما سكتت شهوته طلقت لأن مقصوده في الدخول كان قضاء الشهوة وقد فات . وفي الولوالجية : البول لا يقطع الفور والصلاة إذا خاف خروج وقتها كذلك. وهو قول الحسن بن زياد وبه يفتى. وقال نصير : الصلاة تقطع الفور. وستأتي مسائل الفور في آخر باب اليمين على الخروج والدخول إن شاء الله تعالى. ومما يناسب مسألة أن الصلاة لا تقطع الفور ما في الفتاوى الصيرفية : حلف بالطلاق ليصلين الظهر في مسجده فذهب إلى موضع لو يجيء تفوته الصلاة وإلا لا قال : يصليها في وقته وتطلق. ثم رقم بعلامة ب د أن هذا في الواحدة، أما في الثلاث فيصلي في مسجده ا هـ . وقيد باقتصاره في التعليق على عدم التطليق لأنه لو قال إذا طلقتك فأنت طالق وإذا لم أطلقك فأنت طالق فمات قبل أن يطلق وقع عليها طلاقان، لأنه لما مات قبل التطليق حنث في اليمين الثانية فيقع عليها طلاق، وهذا الطلاق يصلح شرطاً في اليمين. قوله (وهذا الطلاق يصلح شرطاً في اليمين) تأمله مع قوله الآتي ولو قال كلما طلقتك فأنت
One of them: You are divorced as long as I have not divorced you; you are divorced, this divorce occurred, you are such. For example: the day I marry you, from the hour, the day not, connecting, you are divorced, goodness as will come. And like when, time, where, and day. So if he said 'when I have not divorced you' and he had no intention, she is divorced when he becomes silent. Likewise 'time I have not divorced you', 'where I have not divorced you', 'day I have not divorced you' if it is with the jussive 'lam'. But if it is with the negative 'la' like 'time I do not divorce you' or 'when I do not divorce you' with the negative letter 'la', she is not divorced until six months pass. The difference between the two letters is that 'lam' turns the present tense into past with negation, and there existed a time in which he did not divorce her, so it occurred; and the word 'la' is for the future mostly. If he has no intention, it does not occur immediately. What is intended by 'when' is six months because it is the middle of its uses, and forty years and six months in the verse 'So exalted is Allah when you enter the evening and when you become morning' [Ar-Rum:17], 'Has there come upon man a period of time' [Ad-Dahr:1], 'It gives its fruit every season by permission of its Lord' [Ibrahim:25]. And time is like 'when' because they are similar in usage. If he said 'I divorce you', she is not divorced until a full day passes, all from al-Muhit. As for 'where', it is for place, and many places in which he did not divorce her. Likewise in Fath al-Qadir: it is as if he said 'You are divorced in a place where I have not divorced you.' And it is mentioned in al-Mughni that al-Akhfash considered it for time as well, so no problem. It is restricted by what was mentioned because if he said 'Whenever I have not divorced you, you are divorced' and he became silent, three divorces occur successively, not all at once, because it necessitates generality of individuals, not generality of simultaneity. If she is not consummated, she becomes irrevocably divorced with only one. It is restricted by absolute time because if he restricted it with nonexistence, like saying 'If you do not enter the house for a year, you are divorced' and the year passed before entry, she is divorced as in ila'. Likewise in al-Bada'i'. His statement: (And with 'if I have not divorced you' or 'if I have not divorced you' or 'if I have not divorced you' [using 'idha'], not [divorce occurs] until one of them dies) i.e., divorce does not occur except with the death of one of them before the pronouncement of divorce, in the absence of intention and indication of immediacy, because the condition is that he does not divorce her, and that is not realized except by despair of life, which is in the last indivisible part of life. As for his death, it is apparent, and the earlier scholars did not estimate it; rather they said: she is divorced just before his death. If she is consummated, she inherits from him by the ruling of flight even if the divorce is triple; otherwise she does not inherit him. He indicated by his statement 'by the death of one of them' that her death is like his death. Al-Hidayah deemed it correct, and it is not refuted by what if he said 'If I do not enter the house, you are divorced' where it occurs by his death, not by her death, because he can enter after her death, so despair is not realized by her death, so divorce does not occur. As for the divorce, despair is realized by her death due to lack of locality, and if we rule that it occurs just before her death, the husband does not inherit from her because she became irrevocably divorced just before death, so no marital relationship remains at the time of death. We only ruled by irrevocability even if the suspended [divorce] is explicit, because of the absence of waiting period like the non-consummated, because the assumption is that the occurrence is in the last indivisible part and nothing follows but death, and it becomes apparent. Hence the author made the occurrence by death even though it is just before it. And it has become apparent that the lack of inheritance from her is absolute, whether she is consummated or not, triple or one. And by this it becomes clear that the commentator's restriction of its absence to non-consummation or triple is incorrect. And the author's equating 'in' and 'idha' is the madhhab of Abu Hanifa; for in his view, 'idha' when used as a conditional particle, is a pure conditional letter because its pure conditional linking is specific and is among the meanings of letters, and a word may be a letter or a noun. Since it is for condition and time, divorce does not occur immediately due to doubt. In the view of the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad], it is like 'mata' for time. The summary is that the Imam based his madhhab on the fact that 'idha' exits from adverbiality and becomes pure condition, which is the saying of some grammarians as mentioned in al-Mughni, but it is mentioned that the majority hold that it is for adverbiality containing the meaning of conditionality and that it does not exit from adverbiality, and this supports their view here. And his proof is estimated in Fath al-Qadir. And it is not refuted against Abu Hanifa [the case] 'You are divorced if you wish' where he agreed with them that it is like 'mata' and thus the matter does not leave her hand; if it were like 'in', the matter would leave her hand due to doubt about exit after the realization of entry. And an objection was raised against him that the occurrence of doubt in conditionality and adverbiality necessitates its occurrence in permissibility and impermissibility immediately, so it should be deemed impermissible in preference to the impermissible as they said. And it was answered that doubt is nothing; rather that necessitates the conflict of the evidence of impermissibility with the evidence of permissibility, so the precaution is to act on the evidence of impermissibility. But here, if we considered impermissibility, we would not act on evidence but on doubt. And we restricted [the ruling] by absence of intention because if he intended by 'idha' the meaning of 'mata', he is believed in agreement, both judicially and religiously, because he imposes a stricter condition on himself. Likewise if he intended by 'idha' the meaning of 'in' according to their view. And it should be that in their view, he is believed only religiously because in their view it is apparent for adverbiality, and conditionality is a probability, so the judge does not believe him. And we restricted by absence of indication of immediacy because if an indication of it exists, it is acted upon. Hence in al-Qunyah: if she said to him 'Divorce me' and he said 'If I have not divorced you', it occurs immediately. And this restriction was added in al-Mubtagha [with a dot] and he said: if he said to her 'If you do not inform me of such-and-such, you are divorced', it is forever if there is no indication of immediacy. End quote. And he was followed in Fath al-Qadir and said: it is a good restriction. And hence they said: if he wanted to have intercourse with his wife and she did not comply, and he said 'If you do not enter the house with me, you are divorced', and she entered after his desire subsided, she is divorced because his intention in entering was to fulfill desire and it has passed. And in al-Walwaljiyyah: urinating does not break immediacy, and prayer if he fears its time will exit is likewise. This is the saying of al-Hasan ibn Ziyad and it is given fatwa. And Nasir said: prayer breaks immediacy. The issues of immediacy will come at the end of the chapter on oath of exit and entry, God willing. And related to the issue that prayer does not break immediacy is what is in al-Fatawa al-Sayrafiyyah: he swore by divorce to pray Dhuhr in his mosque, then he went to a place where if he does not come [in time], he will miss the prayer, otherwise no. He said: he prays it at its time and his wife is divorced. Then he marked with a sign 'BD' that this is for one [divorce]; as for three, he prays in his mosque. End quote. And it is restricted by his limiting the suspension to not divorcing, because if he said 'If I divorce you, you are divorced' and 'If I do not divorce you, you are divorced' and he died before divorcing, two divorces occur upon her, because when he died before pronouncing divorce, he broke the second oath, so a divorce occurs upon her, and this divorce can serve as a condition for the oath. His statement (And this divorce can serve as a condition for the oath) ponder with his following statement: 'And if he said: Whenever I divorce you, you are...'

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq is a detailed commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq concerning conditional divorce. The author discusses various formulations of the phrase 'if I have not divorced you' (ma lam utallik). He explains that with the particle 'lam' (jussive), the condition refers to the past, so if he says 'when I have not divorced you,' she is divorced immediately upon his silence because there was a time he did not divorce her. With 'la' (negative for future), the condition looks forward, so she is not divorced until six months pass, as 'when' (hin) typically means six months in common usage, supported by Quranic verses. The author also covers temporal and spatial words like 'time', 'where', 'day', noting that 'where' is for place but some grammarians also allow time. For 'if I have not divorced you' (in lam utallik), the ruling is that divorce does not occur until one of them dies, because despair of life is the only way to certainly know he will never divorce her. The divorce is considered to occur just before death, and inheritance rules are affected. The author then presents the intra-Hanafi dispute about the particle 'idha' (if/when). Abu Hanifa holds that 'idha' is purely conditional like 'in', so with doubt about whether it is time or condition, divorce does not occur immediately. The two Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold it is for time like 'mata', so it implies immediacy. The author mentions grammatical arguments and then discusses the role of intention and indications of immediacy (fawr). He clarifies that if there is contextual evidence of immediacy, it is acted upon, and gives examples about prayer and urination interrupting immediacy. Finally, he discusses a case with two conditional statements that result in two divorces upon death. This passage is crucial for understanding how Hanafi fiqh handles conditional divorce with nuanced linguistic analysis, and shows the interplay of grammar, intention, and legal maxims.

Key Terms
لم (lam): Jussive particle that negates the past, turning present tense into past meaning. In conditional divorce, 'lam' indicates a past time frame.
لا (la): Negative particle for future tense. In conditional divorce, 'la' indicates a future time frame, so divorce does not occur until a period passes.
حين (hin): Temporal word meaning 'when'. In conditional divorce, default period is six months unless otherwise specified.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man says to his wife 'زمان لا أطلقك' without any intention, after how long is she divorced?
A) Immediately
B) After six months
C) After one year
D) Upon his death

Key Takeaway
The particle used in a conditional divorce determines the time frame; 'lam' triggers immediate effect, 'la' delays by six months, and 'in lam' delays until death.

Talaq — Divorce
advanced
10 min
The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on 'Idha' and the Principle of Immediacy (Al-Fawr)
Explains the dispute over 'idha' and the rules of fawr (immediacy).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the particle 'idha' in conditional divorce, and apply the rules of al-fawr (immediacy) to determine when divorce occurs.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa treats 'idha' as pure condition (like 'in'), so divorce is doubtful; the Sahibayn treat it as temporal (like 'mata'), so it is immediate – and contextual indications of immediacy override both.

The simple word 'if' or 'when' carries enormous legal weight. In Hanafi law, the meaning of 'idha' split the school's founders. This lesson examines that split and the practical rule of immediacy that helps resolve it, showing how contextual clues like the wife's demand or the husband's purpose can make the condition effective at once.

Guided Path
1
Identify the particle and the dispute
The particle 'idha' can mean 'if' (pure condition) or 'when' (time). Abu Hanifa chooses 'if'; the Sahibayn choose 'when'. This grammatical ambiguity is the root of the legal difference.
The dispute is intra-Hanafi; both views are based on valid grammatical analyses.
2
Apply Abu Hanifa's view
Under Abu Hanifa, since 'idha' is doubtful between condition and time, the divorce does not occur immediately. The marriage continues until one spouse dies (like 'in lam utallik').
Abu Hanifa argues that doubt prevents a ruling of immediate divorce.
3
Apply the Sahibayn's view
Under the Sahibayn, 'idha' is primarily temporal, so the condition is interpreted as 'when'. Divorce occurs immediately upon the utterance of the condition.
The Sahibayn hold that the temporal meaning is the grammatically stronger view.
4
Consider intention and contextual indication (fawr)
Regardless of the default view, if the husband intends a specific meaning (like 'mata' for time or 'in' for condition), his intention is honored. Also, if there is a contextual indication of immediacy (e.g., the wife asks for divorce, or the husband's purpose is clear), the divorce occurs immediately even under Abu Hanifa's view.
The mufta bihi position is cautious: it considers intention and context.
5
Recognize acts that break immediacy
Immediacy (fawr) can be broken by certain acts. According to al-Hasan ibn Ziyad, urination does not break immediacy, and prayer, if he fears missing its time, also does not. But Nasir says prayer does break it. The fatwa is on the side of not breaking except by clear interruption.
This shows the practical application of fawr in daily life.

إشكال. وقيد بما ذكر لأنه لو قال كلما لم أطلقك فأنت طالق وسكت يقع الثلاث متتابعاً لا جملة لأنها تقتضي عموم الانفراد لا عموم الاجتماع، فإن لم تكن مدخولاً بها بانت بواحدة فقط . وقيد بمطلق الوقت لأنه لو قيده مع العدم كأن قال إن لم تدخلي الدار سنة فأنت طالق فمضت السنة قبل الدخول طلقت كما في الإيلاء. كذا في البدائع. قوله : (وفي إن لم أطلقك أو إذا لم أطلقك أو إذا ما لم أطلقك لا حتى يموت أحدهما) أي لا يقع الطلاق إلا بموت أحدهما قبل التطليق عند عدم النية ودلالة الفور لأن الشرط أن لا يطلقها وذلك لا يتحقق إلا باليأس عن الحياة وهو في آخر جزء من أجزاء الحياة. أما في موته فظاهر ولم يقدره المتقدمون بل قالوا : تطلق قبيل موته، فإن كانت مذخولاً بها ورثته بحكم الفرار وإن كان الطلاق ثلاثاً وإلا لا ترثه. وأشار بقوله «بموت أحدهما» أن موتها كموته. وصححه في الهداية، ولا يرد عليه ما لو قال إن لم أدخل الدار فأنت طالق حيث يقع بموته لا بموتها لأنه يمكنه الدخول بعد موتها فلا يتحقق اليأس بموتها فلا يقع الطلاق، أما الطلاق فإنه يتحقق اليأس عنه بموتها لعدم المحلية، وإذا حكمنا بوقوعه قبيل موتها لا يرث منها الزوج لأنها بانت قبيل الموت فلم يبق بينهما زوجية حال الموت. إنما حكمنا بالبينونة وإن كان المعلق صريحاً لانتفاء العدة كغير المدخول بها لأن الفرض أن الوقوع في آخر جزء لا يتجزى فلم يله إلا الموت وبه تبين ولذا جعل المصنف الوقوع بالموت وإن كان قبيله وقد ظهر أن عدم إرثه منها مطلق سواء كانت مدخولاً بها أو لا، ثلاثاً أو واحدة، وبه تبين أن تقييد الشارح عدمه بعدم الدخول أو الثلاث غير صحيح، وتسوية المصنف بين «إن» و «إذا» مذهب أبي حنيفة فهي عنده إذا جوزي بها حرف لمجرد الشرط لأن مجرده ربط خاص وهو من معاني الحروف، وقد تكون الكلمة حرفاً أو اسماً، فلما كانت للشرط والوقت لم يقع الطلاق للحال بالشك، وعندهما كمتى للوقت. وحاصله أن الإمام بنى مذهبه على إن «إذا» تخرج عن الظرفية وتكون لمحض الشرط وهو قول بعض النحاة كما ذكره في المغني لكن ذكر أن الجمهور على أنها للظرفية متضمنة معنى الشرطية وأنها لا تخرج عن الظرفية وهو مرجح لقولهما هنا، وقدر حجه في فتح القدير. ولا يرد على أبي حنيفة أنت طالق إذا شئت حيث وافقهما أنها كمتى فلا يخرج الأمر من يدها ولو كانت كـ«إن الخرج الأمر من يدها الشك الخروج بعد تحقق الدخول واعترض عليه بأن وقوع الشك في الشرطية والظرفية يوجب وقوعه في الحل والحرمة في الحال فكان ينبغي أن تحرم تقديماً للمحرم كما قالا. وأجيب بأن الشك لا شيئاً إنما ذلك يوجب تعارض دليل الحرمة مع دليل الحل فالاحتياط العمل بدليل الحرمة، أما هنا لو اعتبرنا الحرمة لم نعمل بدليل بل بالشك. وقيدنا بعدم النية لأنه لو نوى بـ«إذا» معنى متى» صدق اتفاقاً قضاء وديانة لتشديده على نفسه، وكذا إذا نوى بـ«إذا» معنى «إن» على قولهما. وينبغي أن يصدق عندهما ديانة فقط لأنها عندهما ظاهرة في الظرفية والشرطية احتمال فلا يصدقه القاضي. وقيدنا بعدم دلالة الفور لأنه لو قامت دلالة عليه عمل بها، ولذا قال في القنية : لو قالت له طلقني فقال إن لم أطلقك يقع على الفور. وقد زاد هذا القيد في المبتغى بالمعجمة فقال : لو قال لها إن لم تخبريني بكذا فأنت طالق فهو على الأبد إن لم يكن ثمة ما يدل على الفور ا.هـ. وتبعه عليه في فتح القدير وقال: إنه قيد حسن. ومن ثم قالوا: لو أراد أن يجامع امرأته فلم تطاوعه فقال إن لم تدخلي البيت معي فأنت طالق فدخلت بعد ما سكتت شهوته طلقت لأن مقصوده في الدخول كان قضاء الشهوة وقد فات . وفي الولوالجية : البول لا يقطع الفور والصلاة إذا خاف خروج وقتها كذلك. وهو قول الحسن بن زياد وبه يفتى. وقال نصير : الصلاة تقطع الفور. وستأتي مسائل الفور في آخر باب اليمين على الخروج والدخول إن شاء الله تعالى. ومما يناسب مسألة أن الصلاة لا تقطع الفور ما في الفتاوى الصيرفية : حلف بالطلاق ليصلين الظهر في مسجده فذهب إلى موضع لو يجيء تفوته الصلاة وإلا لا قال : يصليها في وقته وتطلق. ثم رقم بعلامة ب د أن هذا في الواحدة، أما في الثلاث فيصلي في مسجده ا هـ . وقيد باقتصاره في التعليق على عدم التطليق لأنه لو قال إذا طلقتك فأنت طالق وإذا لم أطلقك فأنت طالق فمات قبل أن يطلق وقع عليها طلاقان، لأنه لما مات قبل التطليق حنث في اليمين الثانية فيقع عليها طلاق، وهذا الطلاق يصلح شرطاً في اليمين. قوله (وهذا الطلاق يصلح شرطاً في اليمين) تأمله مع قوله الآتي ولو قال كلما طلقتك فأنت
One of them: You are divorced as long as I have not divorced you; you are divorced, this divorce occurred, you are such. For example: the day I marry you, from the hour, the day not, connecting, you are divorced, goodness as will come. And like when, time, where, and day. So if he said 'when I have not divorced you' and he had no intention, she is divorced when he becomes silent. Likewise 'time I have not divorced you', 'where I have not divorced you', 'day I have not divorced you' if it is with the jussive 'lam'. But if it is with the negative 'la' like 'time I do not divorce you' or 'when I do not divorce you' with the negative letter 'la', she is not divorced until six months pass. The difference between the two letters is that 'lam' turns the present tense into past with negation, and there existed a time in which he did not divorce her, so it occurred; and the word 'la' is for the future mostly. If he has no intention, it does not occur immediately. What is intended by 'when' is six months because it is the middle of its uses, and forty years and six months in the verse 'So exalted is Allah when you enter the evening and when you become morning' [Ar-Rum:17], 'Has there come upon man a period of time' [Ad-Dahr:1], 'It gives its fruit every season by permission of its Lord' [Ibrahim:25]. And time is like 'when' because they are similar in usage. If he said 'I divorce you', she is not divorced until a full day passes, all from al-Muhit. As for 'where', it is for place, and many places in which he did not divorce her. Likewise in Fath al-Qadir: it is as if he said 'You are divorced in a place where I have not divorced you.' And it is mentioned in al-Mughni that al-Akhfash considered it for time as well, so no problem. It is restricted by what was mentioned because if he said 'Whenever I have not divorced you, you are divorced' and he became silent, three divorces occur successively, not all at once, because it necessitates generality of individuals, not generality of simultaneity. If she is not consummated, she becomes irrevocably divorced with only one. It is restricted by absolute time because if he restricted it with nonexistence, like saying 'If you do not enter the house for a year, you are divorced' and the year passed before entry, she is divorced as in ila'. Likewise in al-Bada'i'. His statement: (And with 'if I have not divorced you' or 'if I have not divorced you' or 'if I have not divorced you' [using 'idha'], not [divorce occurs] until one of them dies) i.e., divorce does not occur except with the death of one of them before the pronouncement of divorce, in the absence of intention and indication of immediacy, because the condition is that he does not divorce her, and that is not realized except by despair of life, which is in the last indivisible part of life. As for his death, it is apparent, and the earlier scholars did not estimate it; rather they said: she is divorced just before his death. If she is consummated, she inherits from him by the ruling of flight even if the divorce is triple; otherwise she does not inherit him. He indicated by his statement 'by the death of one of them' that her death is like his death. Al-Hidayah deemed it correct, and it is not refuted by what if he said 'If I do not enter the house, you are divorced' where it occurs by his death, not by her death, because he can enter after her death, so despair is not realized by her death, so divorce does not occur. As for the divorce, despair is realized by her death due to lack of locality, and if we rule that it occurs just before her death, the husband does not inherit from her because she became irrevocably divorced just before death, so no marital relationship remains at the time of death. We only ruled by irrevocability even if the suspended [divorce] is explicit, because of the absence of waiting period like the non-consummated, because the assumption is that the occurrence is in the last indivisible part and nothing follows but death, and it becomes apparent. Hence the author made the occurrence by death even though it is just before it. And it has become apparent that the lack of inheritance from her is absolute, whether she is consummated or not, triple or one. And by this it becomes clear that the commentator's restriction of its absence to non-consummation or triple is incorrect. And the author's equating 'in' and 'idha' is the madhhab of Abu Hanifa; for in his view, 'idha' when used as a conditional particle, is a pure conditional letter because its pure conditional linking is specific and is among the meanings of letters, and a word may be a letter or a noun. Since it is for condition and time, divorce does not occur immediately due to doubt. In the view of the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad], it is like 'mata' for time. The summary is that the Imam based his madhhab on the fact that 'idha' exits from adverbiality and becomes pure condition, which is the saying of some grammarians as mentioned in al-Mughni, but it is mentioned that the majority hold that it is for adverbiality containing the meaning of conditionality and that it does not exit from adverbiality, and this supports their view here. And his proof is estimated in Fath al-Qadir. And it is not refuted against Abu Hanifa [the case] 'You are divorced if you wish' where he agreed with them that it is like 'mata' and thus the matter does not leave her hand; if it were like 'in', the matter would leave her hand due to doubt about exit after the realization of entry. And an objection was raised against him that the occurrence of doubt in conditionality and adverbiality necessitates its occurrence in permissibility and impermissibility immediately, so it should be deemed impermissible in preference to the impermissible as they said. And it was answered that doubt is nothing; rather that necessitates the conflict of the evidence of impermissibility with the evidence of permissibility, so the precaution is to act on the evidence of impermissibility. But here, if we considered impermissibility, we would not act on evidence but on doubt. And we restricted [the ruling] by absence of intention because if he intended by 'idha' the meaning of 'mata', he is believed in agreement, both judicially and religiously, because he imposes a stricter condition on himself. Likewise if he intended by 'idha' the meaning of 'in' according to their view. And it should be that in their view, he is believed only religiously because in their view it is apparent for adverbiality, and conditionality is a probability, so the judge does not believe him. And we restricted by absence of indication of immediacy because if an indication of it exists, it is acted upon. Hence in al-Qunyah: if she said to him 'Divorce me' and he said 'If I have not divorced you', it occurs immediately. And this restriction was added in al-Mubtagha [with a dot] and he said: if he said to her 'If you do not inform me of such-and-such, you are divorced', it is forever if there is no indication of immediacy. End quote. And he was followed in Fath al-Qadir and said: it is a good restriction. And hence they said: if he wanted to have intercourse with his wife and she did not comply, and he said 'If you do not enter the house with me, you are divorced', and she entered after his desire subsided, she is divorced because his intention in entering was to fulfill desire and it has passed. And in al-Walwaljiyyah: urinating does not break immediacy, and prayer if he fears its time will exit is likewise. This is the saying of al-Hasan ibn Ziyad and it is given fatwa. And Nasir said: prayer breaks immediacy. The issues of immediacy will come at the end of the chapter on oath of exit and entry, God willing. And related to the issue that prayer does not break immediacy is what is in al-Fatawa al-Sayrafiyyah: he swore by divorce to pray Dhuhr in his mosque, then he went to a place where if he does not come [in time], he will miss the prayer, otherwise no. He said: he prays it at its time and his wife is divorced. Then he marked with a sign 'BD' that this is for one [divorce]; as for three, he prays in his mosque. End quote. And it is restricted by his limiting the suspension to not divorcing, because if he said 'If I divorce you, you are divorced' and 'If I do not divorce you, you are divorced' and he died before divorcing, two divorces occur upon her, because when he died before pronouncing divorce, he broke the second oath, so a divorce occurs upon her, and this divorce can serve as a condition for the oath. His statement (And this divorce can serve as a condition for the oath) ponder with his following statement: 'And if he said: Whenever I divorce you, you are...'
Source pages p. 477

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq is a detailed commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq concerning conditional divorce. The author discusses various formulations of the phrase 'if I have not divorced you' (ma lam utallik). He explains that with the particle 'lam' (jussive), the condition refers to the past, so if he says 'when I have not divorced you,' she is divorced immediately upon his silence because there was a time he did not divorce her. With 'la' (negative for future), the condition looks forward, so she is not divorced until six months pass, as 'when' (hin) typically means six months in common usage, supported by Quranic verses. The author also covers temporal and spatial words like 'time', 'where', 'day', noting that 'where' is for place but some grammarians also allow time. For 'if I have not divorced you' (in lam utallik), the ruling is that divorce does not occur until one of them dies, because despair of life is the only way to certainly know he will never divorce her. The divorce is considered to occur just before death, and inheritance rules are affected. The author then presents the intra-Hanafi dispute about the particle 'idha' (if/when). Abu Hanifa holds that 'idha' is purely conditional like 'in', so with doubt about whether it is time or condition, divorce does not occur immediately. The two Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold it is for time like 'mata', so it implies immediacy. The author mentions grammatical arguments and then discusses the role of intention and indications of immediacy (fawr). He clarifies that if there is contextual evidence of immediacy, it is acted upon, and gives examples about prayer and urination interrupting immediacy. Finally, he discusses a case with two conditional statements that result in two divorces upon death. This passage is crucial for understanding how Hanafi fiqh handles conditional divorce with nuanced linguistic analysis, and shows the interplay of grammar, intention, and legal maxims.

Key Terms
إذا (idha): Particle used conditionally or temporally. In this text, its interpretation is disputed: Abu Hanifa treats it as a pure conditional (like 'in'), while the Sahibayn treat it as temporal (like 'mata').
الفور (al-fawr): Immediacy; contextual indication that the condition must be fulfilled without delay. In conditional oaths, if there is evidence of immediacy, the condition is considered to require immediate performance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, in the phrase 'إذا لم أطلقك فأنت طالق', the particle 'idha' is considered:
A) Pure conditional
B) Temporal
C) Negative
D) Interrogative

Key Takeaway
The particle 'idha' in conditional divorce is ambiguous; Abu Hanifa treats it as pure condition, the Sahibayn as time, and the presence of contextual immediacy (fawr) resolves the ambiguity in practice.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
8 min
The Meaning of 'Idhā' in Conditional Divorce and the Internal Hanafi Dispute
Explaining the legal interpretation of 'idhā' in conditional divorce and its effect on timing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi positions on the interpretation of 'idhā' in conditional divorce and apply the roles of intention and indications of immediacy.

Core Idea
The meaning of 'idhā' in conditional divorce is disputed between pure condition and time, affecting when divorce takes effect.

In conditional divorce (talaq mu'allaq), the word used can determine whether the divorce occurs immediately or upon future fulfillment. The Hanafi school has an internal dispute: Abu Hanifa treats 'idhā' as a pure conditional particle, while his two companions treat it as a time word. This lesson unpacks the grammatical and legal reasoning behind each position, along with the roles of intention and contextual indications.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi positions
Identify the view of Abu Hanifa and the view of the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding the interpretation of 'idhā' in conditional divorce.
Abu Hanifa: 'idhā' is a pure condition particle (like 'in'). Sahibayn: 'idhā' is a time word (like 'matā').
2
Examine the grammatical basis
Understand the linguistic evidence each side relies on. Abu Hanifa follows a minority grammatical opinion that 'idhā' can exit its adverbial meaning to become purely conditional. The Sahibayn follow the majority of grammarians who hold that 'idhā' retains its adverbial function with an embedded condition.
The text references al-Mughni and Fath al-Qadir for these grammatical arguments.
3
Analyze the legal reasoning
Explain the logic behind each position regarding doubt and precaution. Abu Hanifa argues that the ambiguity between condition and time creates doubt, and divorce cannot be based on doubt. The Sahibayn argue that the primary meaning is time, so the default is immediate effect.
Abu Hanifa's response to the objection that doubt should lead to prohibition is that here we would be acting on doubt, not evidence.
4
Apply the qualifications
Explore how intention (niyyah) and indications of immediacy (fawr) affect the ruling. If the husband intends 'idhā' to mean 'matā', he is believed judicially and religiously by all. If he intends it as 'in', he is believed only religiously according to the Sahibayn. If there is an indication of immediacy, even Abu Hanifa agrees the divorce takes effect immediately.
Example: a wife demanding divorce or a clear contextual cue of urgency triggers fawr.
5
Determine the practical consequence
Use an example to see the difference: If a husband says 'If you enter the house, you are divorced' using 'idhā', and she enters later, according to Abu Hanifa the divorce occurs at entry; according to the Sahibayn it occurs immediately upon utterance.
The text also gives a case of a husband wanting intercourse: if the condition is not fulfilled immediately, the purpose is lost and divorce occurs even on delayed fulfillment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
«إذا» مذهب أبي حنيفة فهي عنده إذا جوزي بها حرف لمجرد الشرط لأن مجرده ربط خاص وهو من معاني الحروف، وقد تكون الكلمة حرفاً أو اسماً، فلما كانت للشرط والوقت لم يقع الطلاق للحال بالشك، وعندهما كمتى للوقت. وحاصله أن الإمام بنى مذهبه على إن «إذا» تخرج عن الظرفية وتكون لمحض الشرط وهو قول بعض النحاة كما ذكره في المغني لكن ذكر أن الجمهور على أنها للظرفية متضمنة معنى الشرطية وأنها لا تخرج عن الظرفية وهو مرجح لقولهما هنا، وقدر حجه في فتح القدير. ولا يرد على أبي حنيفة أنت طالق إذا شئت حيث وافقهما أنها كمتى فلا يخرج الأمر من يدها ولو كانت كـ«إن الخرج الأمر من يدها الشك الخروج بعد تحقق الدخول واعترض عليه بأن وقوع الشك في الشرطية والظرفية يوجب وقوعه في الحل والحرمة في الحال فكان ينبغي أن تحرم تقديماً للمحرم كما قالا. وأجيب بأن الشك لا شيئاً إنما ذلك يوجب تعارض دليل الحرمة مع دليل الحل فالاحتياط العمل بدليل الحرمة، أما هنا لو اعتبرنا الحرمة لم نعمل بدليل بل بالشك. وقيدنا بعدم النية لأنه لو نوى بـ«إذا» معنى متى» صدق اتفاقاً قضاء وديانة لتشديده على نفسه، وكذا إذا نوى بـ«إذا» معنى «إن» على قولهما. وينبغي أن يصدق عندهما ديانة فقط لأنها عندهما ظاهرة في الظرفية والشرطية احتمال فلا يصدقه القاضي. وقيدنا بعدم دلالة الفور لأنه لو قامت دلالة عليه عمل بها، ولذا قال في القنية : لو قالت له طلقني فقال إن لم أطلقك يقع على الفور. وقد زاد هذا القيد في المبتغى بالمعجمة فقال : لو قال لها إن لم تخبريني بكذا فأنت طالق فهو على الأبد إن لم يكن ثمة ما يدل على الفور ا.هـ. وتبعه عليه في فتح القدير وقال: إنه قيد حسن. ومن ثم قالوا: لو أراد أن يجامع امرأته فلم تطاوعه فقال إن لم تدخلي البيت معي فأنت طالق فدخلت بعد ما سكتت شهوته طلقت لأن مقصوده في الدخول كان قضاء الشهوة وقد فات .
The madhhab of Abu Hanifa is that 'idhā' is, in his view, a particle for pure condition, because its bare essence is a specific linkage which is among the meanings of particles, and the word can be a particle or a noun. Since it is for condition and time, divorce does not occur immediately due to doubt. In the view of the two (companions), it is like 'matā' for time. The summary is that the Imam built his madhhab on the basis that 'idhā' exits from being an adverb and becomes purely conditional, which is the statement of some grammarians as mentioned in al-Mughni. But he mentioned that the majority hold that it is for adverbality containing the meaning of condition, and that it does not exit from adverbality, and that is preponderant for their view here. And he estimated his evidence in Fath al-Qadir. It is not objected against Abu Hanifa regarding 'you are divorced if you wish' where he agreed with them that it is like 'matā' so the matter does not leave her hand. And if it were like 'in' the matter would leave her hand due to doubt, the exit occurring after the occurrence of consummation. It was objected that the occurrence of doubt regarding conditionality and adverbality necessitates its occurrence regarding permissibility and prohibition immediately, so it should be that prohibition is given precedence as they said. The answer is that doubt is nothing; rather that necessitates the conflict of evidence of prohibition with evidence of permissibility, so precaution is to act by the evidence of prohibition. But here, if we consider prohibition, we are not acting by evidence but by doubt. We qualified it by the absence of intention because if he intended by 'idhā' the meaning of 'matā', he is believed by agreement both judicially and religiously due to his burdening himself. Similarly, if he intended by 'idhā' the meaning of 'in' according to their view. And it should be that he is believed in their view only religiously because in their view it is apparent for adverbality and conditionality is a probability, so the judge does not believe him. We qualified it by the absence of an indication of immediacy because if an indication exists, it is acted upon. Hence it was said in al-Qunyah: If she said to him 'Divorce me' and he said 'If I do not divorce you', it falls immediately. And this qualification was added in al-Mubtagha with the pointed letter: He said: If he says to her 'If you do not inform me such-and-such, you are divorced', it is forever if there is no indication of immediacy. And he followed him in Fath al-Qadir and said: It is a good qualification. And from this they said: If he wanted to have intercourse with his wife and she did not comply, and he said 'If you do not enter the house with me, you are divorced', and she entered after his desire subsided, she is divorced because his purpose in entering was fulfilling desire and it has passed.
Source pages p. 478

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the word 'idhā' in conditional divorce. Abu Hanifa holds that 'idhā' is a pure conditional particle (like 'in'), so the divorce does not take effect immediately upon utterance; it only occurs when the condition is later fulfilled. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that 'idhā' primarily indicates time (like 'matā'), so the divorce occurs immediately at the moment of utterance, unless there is a clear indication of delay. The author explains Abu Hanifa's reliance on a minority grammatical opinion that 'idhā' can exit its adverbial meaning to become purely conditional. The majority of grammarians support the companions, but Abu Hanifa's view is based on the principle that doubt prevents immediate effect. The discussion also covers the role of intention (niyyah): if the husband intends 'idhā' to mean 'matā', he is believed both judicially and religiously according to all; but if he intends it to mean 'in', he is believed only religiously according to the companions. An additional qualification is the presence of an indication of immediacy (fawr). If such an indication exists—for example, a clear contextual cue like a wife demanding immediate divorce—the divorce falls immediately even in Abu Hanifa's view. The text gives an example: a husband who says 'If you do not enter the house with me, you are divorced' while wanting intercourse; if she enters after his desire has passed, she is divorced because his purpose was immediate gratification which is now gone.

Key Terms
إذا (idhā): A word used for conditional divorce; its interpretation as pure condition or time is disputed.
فور (fawr): Immediacy; an indication that the condition must be fulfilled without delay.
نية (niyyah): Intention of the husband regarding the meaning of the word.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, if a husband says 'You are divorced if you enter the house' using 'idhā', and there is no indication of immediacy, when does the divorce take effect?
A) Immediately upon utterance.
B) When she enters the house.
C) Only if she does not enter within a month.
D) It never takes effect.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi dispute over 'idhā' turns on whether it is a pure condition (Abu Hanifa) or a time word with conditional nuance (Sahibayn), with intention and context as decisive factors.

What Breaks the Immediacy (Fawr) in Conditional Divorce
Listing actions that break the immediate expectation of a conditional divorce.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which actions break the immediacy (fawr) in conditional divorce according to the preferred Hanafi position, and explain the intra-madhhab difference.

Core Idea
Urination does not break fawr; prayer does not break it per the fatwa, except in triple divorce cases.

Conditional divorce often requires immediate fulfillment of a condition. But what if a man needs to urinate or pray during that process? The Hanafi school distinguishes between actions that excuse delay and those that do not. This passage clarifies the rule and notes a key exception for triple divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the actions discussed
The text discusses two actions: urination and prayer (when one fears the prayer time will expire). Determine the ruling for each.
2
State the preferred ruling per Hasan ibn Ziyad
Hasan ibn Ziyad holds that neither urination nor prayer breaks fawr. This is the position by which fatwa is given.
The text explicitly says 'by it fatwa is given' (به يفتى).
3
Contrast with Nasir's view
Nasir holds that prayer does break fawr. Note the intra-Hanafi difference but that the fatwa follows Hasan ibn Ziyad.
4
Understand the exception for triple divorce
In cases of triple divorce (three divorces at once), the ruling is stricter: the husband must fulfill the condition in the specific manner (e.g., pray in the mosque) even if it means missing the prayer time, to avoid irrevocable divorce.
The text marks this distinction with 'b d' (ب د).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الولوالجية : البول لا يقطع الفور والصلاة إذا خاف خروج وقتها كذلك. وهو قول الحسن بن زياد وبه يفتى. وقال نصير : الصلاة تقطع الفور. وستأتي مسائل الفور في آخر باب اليمين على الخروج والدخول إن شاء الله تعالى. ومما يناسب مسألة أن الصلاة لا تقطع الفور ما في الفتاوى الصيرفية : حلف بالطلاق ليصلين الظهر في مسجده فذهب إلى موضع لو يجيء تفوته الصلاة وإلا لا قال : يصليها في وقتها وتطلق. ثم رقم بعلامة ب د أن هذا في الواحدة، أما في الثلاث فيصلي في مسجده ا هـ .
And in al-Walwaljiyyah: Urination does not break the immediacy, and prayer if one fears its time will leave (is also like that). And it is the statement of al-Hasan ibn Ziyad and by it fatwa is given. And Nasir said: Prayer breaks the immediacy. The issues of immediacy will come at the end of the chapter on oaths regarding exiting and entering, God willing. And among what is appropriate for the issue that prayer does not break the immediacy is what is in al-Fatawa al-Sayrafiyyah: He swore by divorce to pray zuhr in his mosque, and he went to a place such that if he comes (back) his prayer would be missed, otherwise not. He said: He prays it in its time and she is divorced. Then he marked with the sign 'b d' that this is regarding one divorce; as for three, he prays in his mosque.
Source pages p. 478

Commentary
This passage discusses exceptions to the rule of immediacy (fawr) in conditional divorce. When a husband imposes a condition framed with immediate effect, certain external factors may excuse the delay. The text cites al-Walwaljiyyah stating that urination does not break the immediacy—meaning if a man is in the process of fulfilling the condition but needs to urinate, that does not give him extra time; the condition must still be met without delay. Similarly, if he is praying and fears the prayer time will expire, that also does not break the immediacy according to the preferred view of Hasan ibn Ziyad, and this is the fatwa. However, Nasir holds that performing prayer does break the immediacy. The author notes that further details will come later. An example from al-Fatawa al-Sayrafiyyah illustrates: a man swore by divorce to pray zuhr in his mosque. He went to a location where returning to the mosque would cause him to miss the prayer time. The ruling is that he should pray in time (outside the mosque) and the divorce takes effect (because he failed to fulfill the condition immediately). But if the divorce was triple, he must pray in the mosque to avoid the irrevocable divorce.

Key Terms
فور (fawr): Immediacy; the requirement that a condition be fulfilled without delay.
قطع الفور (qat' al-fawr): Breaking the immediacy; allowing a delay in fulfilling the condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view (fatwa) mentioned in the text, does performing the prayer with fear of its time expiring break the immediacy (fawr) of a conditional divorce?
A) Yes, it breaks the immediacy.
B) No, it does not break the immediacy.
C) It depends on whether the prayer is obligatory or voluntary.
D) It breaks only if the condition involves a mosque.

Key Takeaway
The default is that fawr is not broken by urination or prayer, though there is a difference of opinion on prayer.

Two Opposite Conditional Oaths and Death
Rulings on multiple conditional divorce oaths and death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the number of divorces when a husband makes two opposite conditional oaths and dies before divorcing, and explain why the order matters.

Core Idea
The order of conditional oaths determines how many divorces occur upon death.

This lesson examines the interaction of two conditional divorce oaths—one on divorce and one on non-divorce—when the husband dies before acting. The ruling hinges on the principle that conditions are prospective, and a divorce itself can become a new condition for another oath.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Oaths
The husband says: 'If I divorce you, you are divorced' and 'If I do not divorce you, you are divorced'. Note the order.
The order affects the outcome.
2
Apply the Death Scenario
He dies before divorcing her. Determine which oath is violated first.
Death is considered a failure to perform, so the second oath (non-divorce) is violated.
3
Analyze the First Oath
The divorce from the second oath becomes a condition for the first oath, causing it to be violated as well.
The condition of the first oath is 'if I divorce you', which now occurred.
4
State the Final Number
Thus two divorces occur. If the order were reversed, only one divorce occurs because the first divorce speech preceded the second oath and cannot be a condition.
Conditions are only for the future.

And it was qualified by its restriction in the suspension to not divorcing, because if he said 'If I divorce you, you are divorced' and 'If I do not divorce you, you are divorced' and he died before divorcing, two divorces occur upon her because when he died before divorcing, he violated the second oath so a divorce occurs upon her, and this divorce qualifies as a condition for the first oath, so he violated both oaths. And if he reversed and said 'If I do not divorce you, you are divorced' and 'If I divorce you, you are divorced' and he died before divorcing, one divorce occurs due to the first oath, and it does not qualify as a condition for the second because it occurred by speech that existed before the second oath, and conditions are regarded for the future, not the past. And if he were impotent ('inan) and a separation was made between them, it does not occur according to the most correct view. The difference is that in ila' the divorce occurs by his speech in reality; in the impotent, it does not, but rather he is made a divorcer by law. Thus in al-Muhit. And in li'an (mutual imprecation), he does not violate according to Abu Yusuf; but according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), he violates. And in khul' (divorce by redemption), he violates. If an agent divorced or manumitted, he violates, whether the agency was before or after the oath. Similarly if he said 'Free yourself' and 'Divorce yourself'. Thus in al-Muhit. And in it: If he said to her 'Whenever my divorce befalls you, you are divorced' and he divorced her once, three occur because he made the occurrence of divorce upon her the condition of violation, and divorce occurred upon her twice after the oath—once by the divorce and once by the violation—so the third occurred by the occurrence of the second, because 'whenever' requires repetition of the result with repetition of the condition. And if he said 'Whenever I divorce you, you are divorced' and then he divorced her, two occur because he made divorcing her the condition of violation, and it occurred only once, so one occurred by effectuation and another by violation, and the oath remains binding because it was concluded with a particle of repetition. End. And in Sharh al-Talkhis from the chapter on divorce with or without violation: If he said 'If I divorce Zaynab, then Amrah is divorced; if I divorce Amrah, then Hamadah is divorced; if I divorce Hamadah, then Zaynab is divorced' and he divorced the first, the other is not divorced because the middle one was divorced by a wording that preceded the oath of the other, and the condition is future, not past. Similarly, if he divorced the middle one, the first is not divorced because the other was divorced by a wording that preceded the oath of the first, as in al-Muhit. Unlike 'If my divorce occurs', because the condition is occurrence and it has been delayed. Its parallel is 'If I stop (divorce) or utter (it)'. And if he divorced the other, the middle one is divorced because the divorce of the first is delayed after the oath of the middle one. And if he said 'If I divorce Hamadah, then Bushayrah (is divorced); if I divorce Bushayrah, then Zaynab (is divorced)' and he divorced Hamadah, Bushayrah is divorced. And if he divorced Bushayrah, all are divorced except Hamadah, and the particle is as before. Hence, if he made Zaynab the result for Amrah and then reversed, Zaynab is divorced twice if he divorced her, and once if he divorced Amrah. And if he divorced one of them and died before consummation and clarification, then regarding the triple for Amrah...
Source pages p. 479

Commentary
This passage presents a series of complex rulings on multiple conditional divorce statements and their interaction with death and other actions. The key principle is that conditions are prospective, not retroactive. When a husband makes two oaths—one conditional on him divorcing, and one on him not divorcing—and he dies before doing either, the number of divorces depends on the order. If he says 'If I divorce you, you are divorced' and 'If I do not divorce you, you are divorced', dying before divorcing triggers the second oath immediately, and that divorce itself becomes the condition for the first oath, resulting in two divorces. However, if he reverses the order, only one divorce occurs because the first oath is triggered (since he did not divorce), and that divorce (which is a speech) occurred before the second oath and cannot serve as a condition for it. Qadi Khan adds that on the analogy of the two companions' view, the violation occurs as soon as he remains silent (implying an immediate consequence) rather than waiting for death. The text then discusses similar issues with ila' (oath of abstinence from intercourse), where if the condition is 'if I divorce you' and he practices ila', after the waiting period a divorce occurs, which then triggers the condition, resulting in two divorces. For an impotent man, the separation ordered by the judge is not considered a real divorce from the husband, so it does not trigger the condition. The passage also covers li'an (mutual imprecation) where Abu Yusuf holds that it does not count as a violation of the oath, while the two imams hold that it does—this is a reference to a debate about whether li'an is considered a divorce. In khul' (divorce initiated by the wife with compensation), it does count as a divorce and violates the oath. For a third party (fuduli) who performs khul' on behalf of the husband, if the husband authorizes it verbally, it counts; if by action (like paying money), it does not. The text then addresses repeated conditions with 'kullama' (whenever). If a husband says 'Whenever my divorce befalls you, you are divorced' and then divorces her once, three divorces occur because the condition is the occurrence of divorce; after the first divorce, the condition triggers a second (via violation), and that second occurrence triggers the condition again, resulting in a third. If he says 'Whenever I divorce you, you are divorced' and then divorces her once, only two divorces occur—the original and one from violation—because the condition (his divorcing) only happened once, but the consequence of the violation is a single divorce. The final section presents a chain of conditional divorces among multiple wives. For example, if Zaynab is the result for divorcing Amrah, and vice versa, careful analysis of which divorce occurs first determines the outcomes. In a triangular chain, divorcing the first wife does not trigger the others because the condition for the second wife was already met by a prior statement. The text concludes with a case where a man divorces one of three wives and dies before consummation and clarification, leaving uncertainty about which one was intended; the ruling is that the triple divorce applies to Amrah (a specific name given in the chain).

Key Terms
كلما (kullama): Whenever; a conditional particle indicating repetition of the result with repetition of the condition.
الإيلاء (al-ila'): An oath of abstinence from intercourse; if the husband takes an oath not to have intercourse with his wife for four months, a divorce occurs after the period.
اللعان (al-li'an): Mutual imprecation; a divorce procedure when a husband accuses his wife of adultery without witnesses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband says: 'If I divorce you, you are divorced' and 'If I do not divorce you, you are divorced'. He dies before divorcing. How many divorces occur?
A) None, because death cancels all oaths.
B) One divorce from the second oath.
C) Two divorces, one from each oath.
D) Three divorces, because death causes immediate triple divorce.

Key Takeaway
The order of opposite conditional oaths determines whether one or two divorces occur upon death due to the prospective nature of conditions.

Ila', Li'an, Khul', and Agency in Conditional Divorce
Rulings on various divorce mechanisms as conditions for divorce oaths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether ila', li'an, khul', or an agent's action violate a conditional divorce oath, and explain the differences.

Core Idea
Not all actions that cause a divorce trigger a conditional oath; ila' and khul' do, but li'an has a disagreement, and an impotent's separation does not.

This lesson explores how different types of divorce—ila', li'an, khul', and agency—interact with a previously made conditional divorce oath. The rulings depend on whether the divorce is considered the husband's act or a legal construct.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Condition
Assume the husband has said 'If I divorce you, you are divorced' (condition: his divorcing).
We consider cases where a divorce occurs through ila', li'an, khul', or agency.
2
Analyze Ila'
If he practices ila', after four months a divorce occurs by operation of law. This is considered a divorce by his speech (the oath), so it violates the condition.
The ruling is that two divorces occur (the ila' divorce and the conditional one).
3
Analyze Impotence (Inan)
If a judge separates them due to impotence, that separation is not a divorce from the husband, so it does not violate the condition.
The difference is that in ila' the divorce is directly from the husband's words.
4
Analyze Li'an
Abu Yusuf holds that li'an does not count as a divorce for the oath, so no violation. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that it does, so violation occurs.
This is a madhhab dispute within the Hanafi school.
5
Analyze Khul' and Agency
Khul' (divorce initiated by wife with compensation) is a divorce, so it violates. If a third party (fuduli) performs khul', verbal authorization counts; mere action does not.
Also, an agent's divorce or manumission violates the oath, whether the agency was before or after the oath.

الفقيه أبو الليث : لا يحنث في الإيلاء. كذا في المبتغى ولو علق ووجد الشرط، فإن كان التعليق قبل اليمين لا يحنث وإلا حنث لو طلق الوكيل أو أعتق حنث، سواء كان التوكيل قبل اليمين أو بعده، وكذا لو قال أعتق نفسك وطلقي نفسك. كذا في المحيط وفيه : لو قال لها كلما وقع عليك طلاقي فأنت طالق فطلقها واحدة وقع الثلاث لأنه جعل شرط الحنث وقوع الطلاق عليها وقد وقع الطلاق عليها مرتين بعد اليمين مرة بالتطليق ومرة بالحنث فوقعت الثالثة بوقوع الثانية لأن كلما») توجب تكرار الجزاء بتكرار الشرط. ولو قال كلما طلقتك فأنت طالق ثم طلقها يقع ثنتان لأنه جعل شرط الحنث تطليقها ولم يوجد إلا مرة واحدة فوقعت واحدة بالإيقاع وأخرى بالحنث وبقيت اليمين منعقدة لأنها عقدت بحرف التكرار ا هـ . وفي شرح التلخيص من باب الطلاق بحنث أم بغير حنث: لو قال إن طلقت زينب فعمرة طالق، وإن طلقت عمرة فحمادة طالق، وإن طلقت حمادة فزينب طالق فطلقت الأولى لم تطلق الأخرى إذ الوسطى طلقت بلفظ سبق يمين الأخرى والشرط آت لا ماض، وكذا لو طلق الوسطى لم تطلق الأولى إذ الأخرى طلقت بلفظ سبق يمين الأولى كما المحيط بخلاف إن وقع طلاقي إذ الشرط الوقوع وقد تأخر وزانه أن أوقفت أو لفظت وإن طلق الأخرى تطلق الوسطى لتأخر طلاق الأولى عن يمين الوسطى. ولو كان قال إن طلقت حمادة فبشيرة، وإن طلقت بشيرة قزينب وطلق حمادة تطلق بشيرة وإن طلق بشيرة طلقن إلا حمادة والحرف ما مر ، ولهذا لو جعل زينب جزاء لعمرة ثم عكس تطلق زينب مثنى إن طلقها وفرداً إن طلق عمرة. وإن طلق إحداهن ومات قبل الدخول والبيان ففي الثلاث لعمرة
And it was qualified by its restriction in the suspension to not divorcing, because if he said 'If I divorce you, you are divorced' and 'If I do not divorce you, you are divorced' and he died before divorcing, two divorces occur upon her because when he died before divorcing, he violated the second oath so a divorce occurs upon her, and this divorce qualifies as a condition for the first oath, so he violated both oaths. And if he reversed and said 'If I do not divorce you, you are divorced' and 'If I divorce you, you are divorced' and he died before divorcing, one divorce occurs due to the first oath, and it does not qualify as a condition for the second because it occurred by speech that existed before the second oath, and conditions are regarded for the future, not the past. Thus it is mentioned in al-Muntaqa and no disagreement is narrated. Qadi Khan said in his commentary: On the analogy of their view, it should be that death is not waited for, but as soon as he becomes silent, he violates. End. And it was qualified by the condition being not divorcing, because if the condition were divorcing, such as saying 'If I divorce you, you are divorced' and he practiced ila' (oath of abstinence) from her and the period passed, two divorces occur because ila' is a divorce after the period. And if he were impotent ('inan) and a separation was made between them, it does not occur according to the most correct view. The difference is that in ila' the divorce occurs by his speech in reality; in the impotent, it does not, but rather he is made a divorcer by law. Thus in al-Muhit. And in li'an (mutual imprecation), he does not violate according to Abu Yusuf; but according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), he violates. And in khul' (divorce by redemption), he violates. In the khul' of a third party (fuduli), if he authorizes by speech, he violates; if he authorizes by action, he does not violate. Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth said: He does not violate in ila'. Thus in al-Mubtagha. And if he suspended (a condition) and the condition occurred, if the suspension was before the oath, he does not violate; otherwise, he violates. If an agent divorced or manumitted, he violates, whether the agency was before or after the oath. Similarly if he said 'Free yourself' and 'Divorce yourself'. Thus in al-Muhit. And in it: If he said to her 'Whenever my divorce befalls you, you are divorced' and he divorced her once, three occur because he made the occurrence of divorce upon her the condition of violation, and divorce occurred upon her twice after the oath—once by the divorce and once by the violation—so the third occurred by the occurrence of the second, because 'whenever' requires repetition of the result with repetition of the condition. And if he said 'Whenever I divorce you, you are divorced' and then he divorced her, two occur because he made divorcing her the condition of violation, and it occurred only once, so one occurred by effectuation and another by violation, and the oath remains binding because it was concluded with a particle of repetition. End. And in Sharh al-Talkhis from the chapter on divorce with or without violation: If he said 'If I divorce Zaynab, then Amrah is divorced; if I divorce Amrah, then Hamadah is divorced; if I divorce Hamadah, then Zaynab is divorced' and he divorced the first, the other is not divorced because the middle one was divorced by a wording that preceded the oath of the other, and the condition is future, not past. Similarly, if he divorced the middle one, the first is not divorced because the other was divorced by a wording that preceded the oath of the first, as in al-Muhit. Unlike 'If my divorce occurs', because the condition is occurrence and it has been delayed. Its parallel is 'If I stop (divorce) or utter (it)'. And if he divorced the other, the middle one is divorced because the divorce of the first is delayed after the oath of the middle one. And if he said 'If I divorce Hamadah, then Bushayrah (is divorced); if I divorce Bushayrah, then Zaynab (is divorced)' and he divorced Hamadah, Bushayrah is divorced. And if he divorced Bushayrah, all are divorced except Hamadah, and the particle is as before. Hence, if he made Zaynab the result for Amrah and then reversed, Zaynab is divorced twice if he divorced her, and once if he divorced Amrah. And if he divorced one of them and died before consummation and clarification, then regarding the triple for Amrah...
Source pages p. 479

Commentary
This passage presents a series of complex rulings on multiple conditional divorce statements and their interaction with death and other actions. The key principle is that conditions are prospective, not retroactive. When a husband makes two oaths—one conditional on him divorcing, and one on him not divorcing—and he dies before doing either, the number of divorces depends on the order. If he says 'If I divorce you, you are divorced' and 'If I do not divorce you, you are divorced', dying before divorcing triggers the second oath immediately, and that divorce itself becomes the condition for the first oath, resulting in two divorces. However, if he reverses the order, only one divorce occurs because the first oath is triggered (since he did not divorce), and that divorce (which is a speech) occurred before the second oath and cannot serve as a condition for it. Qadi Khan adds that on the analogy of the two companions' view, the violation occurs as soon as he remains silent (implying an immediate consequence) rather than waiting for death. The text then discusses similar issues with ila' (oath of abstinence from intercourse), where if the condition is 'if I divorce you' and he practices ila', after the waiting period a divorce occurs, which then triggers the condition, resulting in two divorces. For an impotent man, the separation ordered by the judge is not considered a real divorce from the husband, so it does not trigger the condition. The passage also covers li'an (mutual imprecation) where Abu Yusuf holds that it does not count as a violation of the oath, while the two imams hold that it does—this is a reference to a debate about whether li'an is considered a divorce. In khul' (divorce initiated by the wife with compensation), it does count as a divorce and violates the oath. For a third party (fuduli) who performs khul' on behalf of the husband, if the husband authorizes it verbally, it counts; if by action (like paying money), it does not. The text then addresses repeated conditions with 'kullama' (whenever). If a husband says 'Whenever my divorce befalls you, you are divorced' and then divorces her once, three divorces occur because the condition is the occurrence of divorce; after the first divorce, the condition triggers a second (via violation), and that second occurrence triggers the condition again, resulting in a third. If he says 'Whenever I divorce you, you are divorced' and then divorces her once, only two divorces occur—the original and one from violation—because the condition (his divorcing) only happened once, but the consequence of the violation is a single divorce. The final section presents a chain of conditional divorces among multiple wives. For example, if Zaynab is the result for divorcing Amrah, and vice versa, careful analysis of which divorce occurs first determines the outcomes. In a triangular chain, divorcing the first wife does not trigger the others because the condition for the second wife was already met by a prior statement. The text concludes with a case where a man divorces one of three wives and dies before consummation and clarification, leaving uncertainty about which one was intended; the ruling is that the triple divorce applies to Amrah (a specific name given in the chain).

Key Terms
الإيلاء (al-ila'): An oath of abstinence from intercourse; if the husband takes an oath not to have intercourse with his wife for four months, a divorce occurs after the period.
اللعان (al-li'an): Mutual imprecation; a divorce procedure when a husband accuses his wife of adultery without witnesses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband conditionally says: 'If I divorce you, you are divorced'. He then practices ila' (oath of abstinence). After four months, how many divorces have occurred?
A) One divorce (from ila')
B) Two divorces (ila' and conditional)
C) None, because ila' is not a divorce
D) Three divorces due to repetition

Key Takeaway
Only divorces directly attributed to the husband's speech or explicit authorization trigger a conditional divorce oath; judicial separations for impotence or li'an (per Abu Yusuf) do not.

Talaq — Divorce

The 'Kullama' Particle and Chain Conditional Divorces
Rulings on kullama and chain conditional divorces.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the number of divorces when a husband uses the word 'kullama' (whenever) in a conditional divorce, and analyze chain conditional divorces among multiple wives.

Core Idea
The particle 'kullama' causes repetition of the result with repetition of the condition, leading to multiple divorces from a single action.

This lesson delves into two advanced topics: first, the use of 'kullama' (whenever) in conditional divorce, which inherently implies repetition; second, chain conditional divorces among multiple wives where each divorce is conditioned on another, creating intricate dependencies.

Guided Path
1
Understand 'Kullama'
The particle 'kullama' means 'whenever' and implies that whenever the condition occurs, the result repeats. It creates a binding oath that remains in effect for each occurrence.
Example: 'Whenever I divorce you, you are divorced' means each time he divorces, an additional divorce occurs.
2
Apply 'Kullama' to Single Action
If he says 'Whenever my divorce befalls you, you are divorced' and divorces her once, the divorce itself is the condition. That divorce triggers the oath (violation), causing a second divorce. That second divorce is a 'falling of divorce' again, triggering a third. So three divorces occur from one verbal divorce.
The condition is 'falling of divorce', which repeats.
3
Apply 'Kullama' to Different Condition
If he says 'Whenever I divorce you, you are divorced' and divorces her once, the condition is his act of divorcing, which occurs only once. So only one additional divorce occurs from the violation (total two), and the oath remains for future divorces.
The condition does not repeat because his act of divorcing is a single event.
4
Analyze Chain Conditional Divorces
When multiple wives are linked in a chain, each condition is tied to the prior wife's divorce. The key is that conditions are prospective; a divorce occurring due to a prior oath cannot serve as a condition for an oath made later in time.
Example: 'If I divorce Zaynab, then Amrah is divorced; if I divorce Amrah, then Hamadah is divorced...'
5
Determine Outcomes in Chains
If he divorces the first wife (Zaynab), the second wife (Amrah) is not divorced because her condition (divorce of Amrah) has not occurred—only Zaynab was divorced. If he divorces the middle wife (Amrah), the first (Zaynab) is not divorced because her condition was the divorce of Amrah, which occurred after Zaynab's oath? Actually careful: The ruling depends on temporal order. The text provides specific cases.
The general rule: a divorce that occurred before the oath cannot be a condition.

Key Terms
كلما (kullama): Whenever; a conditional particle indicating repetition of the result with repetition of the condition.

Calculating Mahr Shares in Conditional Divorce for Multiple Wives
Computing mahr shares for four wives in conditional divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compute mahr shares for multiple wives in a conditional divorce scenario by applying proportional reasoning based on the number of states in which each wife is divorced.

Core Idea
Mahr fractions in conditional divorce are determined by the proportion of states a wife is divorced across all possibilities.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq illustrates a faraid-style calculation of mahr among four wives in a conditional divorce. The method hinges on enumerating all possible 'states' (ahwal) and assigning each wife a share proportional to the number of states in which she is divorced. While the exact conditions are not fully given, the fractions for Amra (5/8) and Hamada (3/8) are derived, and a simpler three-woman summary is provided. Understanding this principle is crucial for correctly distributing mahr in complex talaq mu'allaq scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the wives and possible triggering events
List the four wives (Zainab, Amra, Hamada, fourth unnamed) and note the conditional statements: divorcing one may trigger others. The text later summarises a three-woman case with specific triggers.
The full set of conditions is not in this excerpt, but the principle is independent of the exact triggers.
2
Determine the states (ahwal) in which each wife is divorced
A 'state' is a possible combination of triggered divorces. The text implies a finite set of states (e.g., 8 states for the four wives). For each state, determine which wives are divorced.
The passage does not list all states explicitly; the fractions given (5/8, 3/8) are the result of such enumeration.
3
Compute each wife's mahr fraction as the average of her mahr over all states
If a wife is divorced in a state, she gets her full mahr; if not, she gets 0. The share is the sum of these per-state amounts divided by the total number of states. For Amra, this yields 5/8; for Hamada, 3/8.
This is analogous to calculating an expected value in probability.
4
Apply the three-woman summary as a simpler example
For Zainab, Amra, and Hamada, the triggers are: divorcing Zainab → only Amra divorced; divorcing Amra → only Hamada divorced; divorcing Hamada → Zainab and Amra divorced. There are 3 possible triggering events (states). Thus Amra is divorced in 2 states (when Zainab or Hamada is divorced) → 2/3 mahr; Hamada in 1 state (when Amra is divorced) → 1/3 mahr; Zainab in 1 state (when Hamada is divorced) → 1/3 mahr. Note this does not match the fractions in the four-wife case, showing the number of states differs.
The fractions in the original text (5/8, 3/8) arise from a different set of states (likely 8 in the four-wife scenario).

The calculation uses fractions like half, quarter, five-eighths, etc., to allocate mahr. The passage is dense and requires careful parsing of the conditions.

مهر (mahr): Dower or bridal gift paid by husband to wife; in this context, it is calculated proportionally upon divorce.

Breach of Oath by Conditional Statements (Ta'liq)
Ruling with exceptions on ta'liq as an oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which conditional statements constitute a breach of an oath not to swear and list the six exceptions according to Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
A conditional statement (ta'liq) generally counts as an oath and breaches a vow, unless it falls into one of six specific categories.

In Hanafi fiqh, swearing by divorce is prohibited. When someone swears not to take an oath, any conditional pronouncement (ta'liq) is considered an oath and thus a breach, except in six cases where the statement is interpreted as something else (such as ownership declaration or time specification). This lesson explains the rule and the exceptions based on the commentary of Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Rule
Recognize that a conditional statement (ta'liq) suspending a consequence upon a condition is the pillar (rukn) of an oath. Therefore, if someone swears not to swear, uttering a ta'liq breaches the vow.
The rule applies regardless of whether the consequence is divorce, emancipation, Hajj, or a vow.
2
Distinguish Ta'liq from Idafah
Understand that adding a time element (idafah) like 'tomorrow' makes the pronouncement immediate but delayed in effect, not a suspension. Such statements do not constitute an oath and thus do not breach the vow.
Example: 'You are divorced tomorrow' is not a conditional suspension.
3
Learn the Six Exceptions
Memorize the six cases where ta'liq does not breach: (1) acts of the heart (wish, love, pleasure), (2) coming of the month for those with months, (3) conditional upon divorce, (4) conditional upon payment (kitaba), (5) conditional upon menstruation, (6) conditional upon sunrise.
The rationale is istihsan: interpreting the statement in a way that avoids or minimizes the prohibited swearing by divorce.
4
Apply the Rule and Exceptions
Given a new scenario, first check if it is a conditional statement. If yes, determine if it falls into one of the six exceptions. If not, it constitutes a breach.
Use the principles: acts of heart indicate ownership; time references indicate sunni divorce timing; etc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

حضت حيضة لأنها اسم للكامل منها ولا وجود له إلا بجزء من الطهر فأمكن جعله تفسيراً لطلاق السنة، وكذا عشرين حيضة لأن ما بعدها وقت لطلاق السنة في الجملة إذ لو طلقها في طهر لم يجامعها فيه فإن راجعها وتركها
