Divorce Occurrence by Description: The Principle and Its Proofs
Rule with proofs from Al-Muhit and Al-Khaniyya, and discussion of nullifiers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that divorce occurs by the qualifying description (masdar or sifah) and identify when a divorce pronouncement is invalid due to doubt, exception, or condition.

Core Idea
Divorce hinges on the complete description uttered without doubt, exception, or condition.

In Hanafi law, the wording of a divorce pronouncement is precise. This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq establishes that the legal occurrence of divorce attaches to the descriptive term (like 'irrevocably' or 'for the sunnah') rather than to the mere word 'taliq'. Understanding this distinction is crucial for valid pronouncements and avoiding unintended consequences, especially when conditions, exceptions, or doubts are introduced.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Principle
The passage states that when a divorce is pronounced with a qualifying description (masdar or sifah), the occurrence is attached to that description, not to the active participle 'taliq'.
Example: 'anta taliq al-batta'—occurrence is by 'al-batta'.
2
Examine the Proof from Al-Muhit
If a husband says 'you are divorced for the sunnah' or 'bain' and the wife dies before he says 'for the sunnah' or 'bain', no divorce occurs. The description is part of the enactment, and death prevents its completion.
The description is a condition for the enactment.
3
Examine the Analogy from Emancipation
In Al-Khaniyya, if a man says to his slave 'you are free irrevocably' and the slave dies before 'irrevocably', the slave dies as a slave. This analogy reinforces that the occurrence depends on uttering the full description during the lifetime of the affected person.
Emancipation and divorce both require complete pronouncement.
4
Note the Nullifiers
The passage lists that introducing doubt ('or not divorced'), exception ('except'), or condition ('if it is') after the pronouncement nullifies the enactment. The enactment loses its certainty.
Hanafis require clarity and absence of suspensive elements.
5
Apply the Rule to Variations
Be careful with numerical descriptions like 'first' or 'three'—the occurrence depends on the intended description. The passage notes that 'you are divorced first' is not valid in some views.
The description must be fully uttered before the wife's death or any interruption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن الوقوع أيضاً بالمصدر عند ذكره وكذا الوقوع بالصفة عند ذكرها كما إذا قال أنت طالق البتة كان الوقوع بالبتة حتى لو قال بعدها إن شاء الله متصلاً لا يقع، ولو كان الوقوع باسم الفاعل لوقع ويدل عليه ما في المحيط : لو قال أنت طالق للسنة أو أنت طالق بائن فماتت قبل أن يقول للسنة أو بائن لا يقع شيء لأنه صفة للإيقاع لا للتطليقة فيتوقف الإيقاع على ذكر الصفة، وأنه لا يتصور بعد الموت ا هـ . ويدل عليه بالأولى ما في الخانية من العتق رجل قال لعبده أنت حر البتة فمات العبد قبل أن يقول البتة فإنه يموت عبداً ا.هـ. ومراده من الواحدة مطلق العدد فلو قال أنت طالق ثلاثاً أولاً على الخلاف وقيدنا بالعدد لأنه لو قال أنت طالق أولاً لا يقع في قولهم. وفي المحيط : لو قال أنت طالق أو غير طالق أو أنت طالق أو لا شيء أو أنت طالق أو لا، لا يقع شيء لأنه أدخل الشك في الإيقاع، وكذا لو قال أنت طالق إلا لأن هذا استثناء والإيقاع إذا لحقه استثناء لا يبقى إيقاعاً، وكذا لو قال أنت طالق إن كان أو أنت طالق إن لم يكن أو لولا، لأن هذا شرط، والإيقاع إذا لحقه شرط لم يبق إيقاعاً ا هـ.
Then know that occurrence is also by the verbal noun when it is mentioned, and likewise occurrence is by the adjective when it is mentioned, as if he said 'you are divorced irrevocably (al-batta)', the occurrence is by 'al-batta' such that if he says after it 'if God wills' connected, it does not occur; if occurrence were by the active participle, it would occur. This is supported by what is in Al-Muhit: if he said 'you are divorced for the sunnah' or 'you are divorced bain' and she dies before he says 'for the sunnah' or 'bain', nothing occurs because it is a description of the enactment, not of the divorce itself, so the enactment depends on mentioning the description, and it cannot be conceived after death. End. This is also supported by what is in Al-Khaniyya regarding emancipation: a man said to his slave 'you are free irrevocably (al-batta)' and the slave died before he said 'al-batta', he dies as a slave. End. And he intended by 'one' absolute number; so if he said 'you are divorced three first' there is difference, and we restricted it to number because if he said 'you are divorced first' it does not occur according to their view. And in Al-Muhit: if he said 'you are divorced or not divorced' or 'you are divorced or nothing' or 'you are divorced or no', nothing occurs because he introduced doubt into the enactment; likewise if he said 'you are divorced except' because this is an exception, and if an exception attaches to an enactment, it does not remain an enactment; likewise if he said 'you are divorced if it is' or 'you are divorced if it is not' or 'if only', because this is a condition, and if a condition attaches to an enactment, it does not remain an enactment. End.
Source pages p. 489

Commentary
This passage is a rule-with-proof segment from the commentary. It establishes the principle that when a divorce is pronounced with a qualifying description (like 'bain' or 'li-sunnah'), the legal occurrence (wuqu') attaches to that description, not to the word 'taliq' itself. The author supports this with two key proofs: (1) from Al-Muhit: if a man says 'you are divorced for the sunnah' and the wife dies before he says 'for the sunnah', no divorce occurs because the description is an essential part of the enactment; (2) from Al-Khaniyya: analogously, in emancipation, if a man says 'you are free irrevocably' and the slave dies before 'irrevocably', he dies a slave. This shows that the occurrence hinges on the full description. The passage also discusses that introducing doubt (e.g., 'or not divorced'), exception, or condition into the divorce pronouncement nullifies the enactment because it removes the certainty required for a valid divorce. These rules are consistent with the Hanafi emphasis on clarity and intentionality in divorce formulations.

Key Terms
الوقوع بالمصدر (al-wuqu' bi al-masdar): The divorce occurs by virtue of the verbal noun (e.g., 'al-batta') mentioned, not by the active participle.
الصفة (al-sifah): The adjective or description qualifying the divorce, such as 'bain' or 'li-sunnah'.
الإيقاع (al-iqa'): The enactment of the divorce; the act of pronouncing the divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a man says 'أنت طالق أو لا شيء' (you are divorced or nothing), what is the ruling?
A) The divorce occurs as revocable.
B) Nothing occurs because doubt is introduced.
C) It is valid if he intended divorce.
D) It is considered an exception and thus valid.

Key Takeaway
The occurrence of divorce depends on the complete description uttered without doubt, exception, or condition; these elements void the enactment entirely.

The Ruling of 'Aw' in Oaths
Ruling on the effect of 'aw' in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a person uses 'aw' (or) to connect multiple items in an oath and identify which items are individually binding.

Core Idea
In oaths, 'aw' links the last two items as a pair, while the first item stands alone.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the effect of the particle 'aw' in oath formulations. When a person swears not to speak to someone, using 'aw' between names, the first name is treated separately, while the last two are combined. Understanding this helps in determining when the oath is broken.

Guided Path
1
Identify the structure
Recognize that the oath uses 'aw' between three items (implicitly 'I will not speak to Zaid, or Amr, or Bakr').
The text mentions 'third and second' indicating a sequence of three.
2
Distinguish the first item
Note that speaking to the first alone breaks the oath. This is because the first item has its ruling independently.
3
Understand the combined items
The last two items are linked with 'waw' (and) in effect, meaning they form a conjunctive pair. The oath is not broken by speaking to only one of them.
The text says 'it does not break except by speaking to the last two'.
4
Apply the rule
If the swearer speaks to the first alone, he breaks the oath. If he speaks to only one of the last two, the oath is not broken; he must speak to both.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتخيير إنما هو بين الأولين. وأما في الأيمان فإنما هو جمع بين الثالث والثاني بالواو والأول ثبت له الحكم وحده، فإن كلم الأول وحده حنث ولا يحنث إلا بكلام الأخيرين ولا يحنث بكلام أحدهما، والفرق ما ذكره في التلخيص. وحاصل «أو» في الطلاق إما في أصله كأنت طالق أولاً لا وقوع اتفاقاً أو بعد العدد فكذا عندهما خلافاً لمحمد كأنت طالق واحدة أو لا، أو بين كأنت طالق واحدة أو ثنتين فالبيان إليه في المدخولة وواحدة في غيرها: أو بين امرأتين فطلاق كأنت طالق أو هذه أو بين ثلاث نسوة و أو في الأخيرة فقط طلقت عددين منهم الأولى، والبيان له في الأخريين أو بين ثلاث و «أو» في الثانية فقط وقع على الأخيرة والبيان له في الأوليين ولو بين أربع مكررة بأن ذكر «أو» في الثانية والواو في الثالثة و «أو» في الرابعة طلقت إحدى الأوليين وإحدى الأخريين ولو ذكر الثانية بالواو والثالثة بـ«أو» وكذا الرابعة بالواو طلقت الأولى والأخيرة والبيان إليه في الثانية والثالثة. ولو أدخل «أو» على الثانية فقط فالبيان إليه في الأولى والثانية ووقع على الثالثة والرابعة . وأما المسألة الثانية أعني مع موتي أو مع موتك فلإضافة الطلاق إلى حالة منافية له لأن موته ينافي الأهلية وموتها ينافي المحلية ولا بد من الأهلية في الموقع والمحلية في الموقع عليها إذ المعنى على تعليقه بالموت وإن كانت «مع» للقران بدليل أنت طالق مع دخولك الدار فإنه يتعلق به فاستدعى وقوعه تقدم الشرط وهو الموت فيقع بعد الموت وهو باطل.
And the choice is only between the first two. As for oaths, it is a combination between the third and second with 'waw', and the first alone has its ruling established. So if he speaks to the first alone, he breaks the oath, and he does not break the oath except by speaking to the last two, and he does not break the oath by speaking to one of them. The difference is what was mentioned in al-Talkhis. The summary of 'aw' in divorce: either it is in its origin, like 'you are divorced, first' – no occurrence by agreement; or after a number, similarly according to them (the two) contrary to Muhammad, like 'you are divorced one or not'; or between, like 'you are divorced one or two' – then the clarification is up to him in the case of a consummated marriage, and one in other than her; or between two women, like 'you are divorced or this one'; or among three women with 'aw' in the last only – the first is divorced with two numbers, and clarification is up to him regarding the other two; or among three with 'aw' in the second only – it falls on the last, and clarification is up to him regarding the first two; if it is between four repeatedly by mentioning 'aw' in the second, 'waw' in the third, and 'aw' in the fourth – one of the first two and one of the last two are divorced; if he mentions the second with 'waw', the third with 'aw', and the fourth with 'waw' – the first and last are divorced, and clarification is up to him regarding the second and third; if he enters 'aw' on the second only – clarification is up to him regarding the first and second, and it falls on the third and fourth. As for the second issue, namely 'with my death or with your death', it is because the divorce is added to a state contrary to it, because his death negates capacity and her death negates locality, and capacity is necessary in the one who effects divorce and locality in the one upon whom divorce is effected, since the meaning is that it is suspended on death, even though 'ma'a' indicates conjunction, as evidence by 'you are divorced with your entering the house' – it is attached to it, so its occurrence requires the prior condition, which is death, so it occurs after death, and it is invalid.
Source pages p. 491

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the rulings concerning the particle 'aw' (or) in divorce and oaths. The author first discusses a case of acknowledgment and oath, then moves to the various usages of 'aw' in divorce. In oaths, 'aw' combines the third and second with 'waw' (and), while the first stands alone. Then the author summarizes the meanings of 'aw' in divorce: 1) original use (like 'you are divorced, first') – no divorce occurs by agreement; 2) after a number (like 'you are divorced one or not') – according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, the same as original, but Muhammad differs; 3) between options (like 'one or two') – clarification is up to the husband if the marriage is consummated, otherwise it is one; 4) between women – various intricacies of wording. Finally, the case of 'with my death or your death' is invalid because it adds divorce to a state that negates the capacity or locality required for divorce, and it is a suspended condition that cannot be realized.

Key Terms
أو (aw): The Arabic word 'or' used in oath pronouncements; here it causes the last two items to be conjunct.
حنث (hanth): Breaking an oath, requiring expiation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling on 'aw' in oaths, when does a person break his oath if he swears not to speak to three people?
A) If he speaks to any one of them.
B) If he speaks to the first alone, or to both of the last two.
C) Only if he speaks to all three.
D) Only if he speaks to the first two.

Key Takeaway
In oaths, 'aw' makes the first item independent and the last two a pair.

The Categories of 'Aw' in Divorce
Categories and rulings of 'aw' in divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three categories of 'aw' in divorce and apply the correct ruling for each, including when the husband can clarify his intention.

Core Idea
In divorce, 'aw' can be original, after a number, or between options, each with distinct effects.

This passage systematically breaks down the different uses of the particle 'aw' (or) in divorce pronouncements. The Hanafi scholars have identified several categories, from its original use (where no divorce occurs) to its use between numbers (where the husband may clarify his intent) and between women. Understanding these categories is essential for determining the validity and number of divorces.

Guided Path
1
Identify the category of 'aw'
The text lists three main categories: 'fi aslihi' (original), 'ba'da al-'adad' (after a number), and 'bayn' (between options or women). Determine which category the divorce wording falls into.
Original: e.g., 'you are divorced, first' – no occurrence. After number: e.g., 'one or not' – similarly no occurrence according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (but Muhammad differs). Between: e.g., 'one or two' – husband may clarify if consummated.
2
Apply the ruling for original and after number
For original and after number (according to the two), no divorce occurs by agreement. For after number, Muhammad holds that it is like between options.
Note the intra-Hanafi difference.
3
Handle between options (consummated vs. non-consummated)
If the marriage is consummated, the husband has the right to clarify which number he intended. If not consummated, it is one divorce.
4
Handle between women
If 'aw' is between women, various scenarios arise depending on the pattern of 'aw' and 'waw'. The husband may have clarification rights for some.
The text gives multiple examples with three or four women.
5
Identify invalid conditions
The final case of 'with my death or your death' is invalid because it attaches divorce to a state that negates capacity or locality.
This is a separate ruling about conditional divorce.

Source Text

البيان (al-bayan): Clarification, the husband's right to explain his intention.

Purchase of Wife Dissolves Marriage: Divorce Invalid, No Iddah
Commentary on purchase of wife and its effects on divorce and iddah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a husband's purchase of his slave-wife dissolves the marriage immediately, and explain why a subsequent divorce is invalid and iddah is not required.

Core Idea
Ownership annuls marriage: divorce after purchase is void, and no iddah because wife is instantly permissible.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a unique Hanafi rule: when a husband purchases his own wife (who is a slave), the marriage contract dissolves automatically because ownership and marriage cannot coexist. The text explains that a later divorce is legally impossible, and the wife does not need to observe iddah. It also addresses an objection from Al-Kafi regarding whether the husband could then marry her to another person.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its basis
Read the opening statement: if the husband purchases his wife and then divorces her, the divorce does not occur. The reason given is that divorce requires an existing marriage, but purchase terminates it.
The ruling is specific to a slave wife; a free wife cannot be owned.
2
Analyze the logic of dissolution
The text distinguishes between partial ownership (owning a part) and complete ownership (owning the whole). Both forms are incompatible with the marriage bond. Understand that ownership here is absolute, making the marriage null.
Contrast with other schools that may treat purchase as a form of divorce or maintain the marriage.
3
Examine the iddah ruling
The text states iddah is not obligatory because the wife becomes immediately lawful for the husband. The possibility of intercourse during iddah is self-contradictory.
Iddah is normally required to ascertain pregnancy, but here it is impossible for the wife to be pregnant from the husband while married to him under ownership.
4
Consider the objection from Al-Kafi
The objection argues that if iddah is not needed, the husband could marry her to another person. The text refutes this by stating that the master's command to marry was not to dower his own neck (i.e., the slave's own value), so the slave's act is unauthorized (فضولي).
This introduces the concept of agency that will be explored in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله (فلو اشتراها ثم طلقها لم يقع لأن الطلاق يستدعي قيام النكاح ولا بقاء له المنافي لا من وجه كما في ملك البعض ولا من كل وجه كما في ملك الكل. والعدة غير واجبة فإنه يحل له ويستحيل وجود الوطء حلا مع قيام العدة. كذا في المحيط. وأورد في الكافي على قولهم بعدم وجوب العدة عليها لو اشتراها أنه لا يجوز له التزويج بها من آخر، لأنه أمره بالنكاح لا بإمهار رقبته فكان فضولياً فلم تصر ملكاً للحرة ولا لمولى الأمة، وتسمية المانع ! الرقبة مهراً من العبد لتقدير المهر بها كما لو تزوج امرأة على عبد الغير. وهذا لأن أمر المولى له بالنكاح أمر بالإمهار فينعقد على قيمته وإن كانت أكثر من مهر المثل عنده لأنها أقل جهالة. وقالا : إذا كانت أكثر منه بغبن فاحش لا يصح، وهذه المسألة فريعة التوكيل بالتزويج فإنه لو وكل رجلاً أن يزوجه امرأة بعينها فزوجه إياها بأكثر من مهر المثل جاز ولزمه عنده لأن المطلق يجري على إطلاقه إلا الدليل التقييد، وعندهما لا يلزمه بدلالة العرف
His statement: (If he purchases her then divorces her, the divorce does not occur because divorce requires the existence of marriage and there is no remaining marriage. The contradictory [ownership] is either partial as in owning a part, or complete as in owning the whole. And iddah is not obligatory, for she becomes permissible to him and it is impossible for intercourse to be permissible while iddah exists. Thus in al-Muhit. And it was objected in al-Kafi to their statement that iddah is not obligatory upon her if he purchases her, that it is not permissible for him to marry her to another, because he [the master] commanded him to marry, not to dower his neck, so he was an unauthorized agent, and she did not become the property of the free woman nor of the slave woman's master. And naming the [؟] the neck as a dower from the slave is to estimate the dower by it, as if he married a woman on the dower of a stranger's slave. And this is because the master's command to him to marry is a command to provide a dower, so the marriage is contracted on its value even if it is more than the fair dower according to him [Abu Hanifa] because it is less unknown. And they [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad] said: if it is more than the fair dower by an excessive disparity, it is not valid. And this issue is subsidiary to the agency for marriage. For if he authorized a man to marry him to a specific woman, and he married him to her with more than the fair dower, it is valid and binding according to him, because the absolute [authorization] proceeds on its absoluteness unless there is evidence restricting it. And according to them, it is not binding due to custom.
Source pages p. 493

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the ruling that if a husband purchases his wife, the marriage is dissolved immediately, so any later divorce is ineffective. The commentary explains the reasoning: divorce requires an existing marriage, and ownership of the wife by the husband cancels the marriage. Likewise, iddah is not necessary because the wife becomes lawful to the husband immediately, and the possibility of intercourse during iddah is logically impossible. The text then addresses an objection from al-Kafi: if iddah is not required, can the husband marry her to someone else? The commentary says no, because the master's command to marry was not to dower his own neck (i.e., the slave's own value). The slave's act is unauthorized (فضولي). The text then discusses a subsidiary issue: if a master authorizes a slave to marry a free woman, and the slave agrees to a dower more than the fair dower, Abu Hanifa holds the marriage valid and binding, while the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) require excessive disparity (غبن فاحش) to invalidate. This is derived from the principle of agency: an absolute agent is unrestricted unless evidence restricts him.

Key Terms
شراء الزوج زوجته (shira' al-zawj zawjatahu): The act of a husband purchasing his wife who is a slave, which dissolves the marriage immediately.
عدة (iddah): The waiting period after dissolution of marriage; here deemed non-obligatory because the wife becomes lawful to the husband immediately.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, why is a divorce pronounced after purchasing one's wife invalid?
A) Because divorce requires the wife's consent after purchase.
B) Because the marriage is already dissolved by purchase, so no marriage exists.
C) Because purchase is considered a form of divorce itself.
D) Because the wife becomes a free woman and cannot be divorced.

Key Takeaway
Ownership of a slave-wife by the husband immediately terminates the marriage, rendering any subsequent divorce void and removing the obligation of iddah.

Agency for Marriage: Validity of Excessive Dower Between Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn
Commentary on agency for marriage with excessive dower and the dispute between Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on the validity of a marriage contracted by an agent with a dower excessive beyond the fair dower, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Absolute agency in marriage is unrestricted; excessive dower invalid only if disparity is exorbitant (Sahibayn's view).

This passage extends the earlier discussion on purchase of a wife to the related issue of agency: when a master authorizes his slave to marry a free woman, and the slave agrees to a dower far above the fair dower. Abu Hanifa holds the marriage valid because the authorization is absolute, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad require a limit based on custom. The text explains the reasoning and links it to the general principle of agency in marriage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
The text presents a subsidiary issue (فرعية) of agency for marriage. A master tells his slave to marry a specific woman, and the slave contracts a marriage with a dower much higher than the fair dower.
This issue arises from the earlier objection about the slave marrying a free woman after purchase.
2
Understand Abu Hanifa's position and proof
Abu Hanifa says the marriage is valid and binding because the absolute authorization (إطلاق) must remain absolute unless there is evidence to restrict it. The dower set is valid even if above the fair dower because it reduces uncertainty (أقل جهالة).
The principle: المطلق يجري على إطلاقه إلا بدليل التقييد.
3
Understand the Sahibayn's position and proof
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say the marriage is not valid if the dower exceeds the fair dower by an excessive disparity (غبن فاحش). Their proof is that custom (العرف) indicates the principal would not consent to such excess.
Custom can restrict the absolute nature of the agency.
4
Connect to agency principle
The text explicitly states that this issue is a branch (فرعية) of the general rule of agency for marriage (توكيل بالتزويج). The same difference applies if a principal authorizes an agent to marry him to a specific woman and the agent sets a high dower.
The ruling is not limited to slaves; it applies to any agency for marriage.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Under Abu Hanifa, the marriage is valid and the principal must pay the high dower. Under the Sahibayn, the marriage is invalid (or not binding) if the disparity is excessive.
The text does not specify which view is mufta bihi, but later Hanafi authorities often prefer the Sahibayn's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

His statement: (If he purchases her then divorces her, the divorce does not occur because divorce requires the existence of marriage and there is no remaining marriage. And iddah is not obligatory, for she becomes permissible to him and it is impossible for intercourse to be permissible while iddah exists. Thus in al-Muhit. And it was objected in al-Kafi to their statement that iddah is not obligatory upon her if he purchases her, that it is not permissible for him to marry her to another, because he [the master] commanded him to marry, not to dower his neck, so he was an unauthorized agent, and she did not become the property of the free woman nor of the slave woman's master. For if he authorized a man to marry him to a specific woman, and he married him to her with more than the fair dower, it is valid and binding according to him, because the absolute [authorization] proceeds on its absoluteness unless there is evidence restricting it. And according to them, it is not binding due to custom.
Source pages p. 493

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the ruling that if a husband purchases his wife, the marriage is dissolved immediately, so any later divorce is ineffective. The commentary explains the reasoning: divorce requires an existing marriage, and ownership of the wife by the husband cancels the marriage. Likewise, iddah is not necessary because the wife becomes lawful to the husband immediately, and the possibility of intercourse during iddah is logically impossible. The text then addresses an objection from al-Kafi: if iddah is not required, can the husband marry her to someone else? Key Terms
توكيل بالتزويج (tawkil bi al-tazwij): Appointing an agent to conclude a marriage contract on one's behalf; the agent has authority to set the dower unless restricted.
مهر المثل (mahr al-mithl): The fair dower, determined by the dower of women similar to the bride (e.g., family, status).
غبن فاحش (ghaban fahish): An excessive disparity in value, typically judged by custom. Here, it means the dower is far above the fair dower.

C) Because the dower is always negotiable after marriage.
D) Because the fair dower is not a legal requirement.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, an agent authorized to marry may set any dower; excessive dower invalidates the marriage only if the disparity is exorbitant (Sahibayn's view), which is often the mufta bihi.

Khul' on the Neck of a Slave Woman: Ruling and Dispute
Commentary on khul' with slave's neck as dower.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on khul' when the compensation is the slave woman's own neck, distinguish between free and slave husbands, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute with Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
Khul' with the slave's neck as dower is void, but the divorce still occurs irrevocably.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq tackles a subtle scenario: a master makes khul' of his slave woman from her free husband, using the slave woman herself as the compensation. Is this valid? The text reveals a fascinating legal mechanism: the contract fails regarding the dower, yet the divorce takes effect. Understanding this requires grasping the distinction between essential and non-essential elements in khul' and the nature of kinayah expressions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the core contradiction
Recognize that the husband is free, and the dower is the slave's own neck. This creates a contradiction: if khul' were valid, the husband would own the slave at the moment of divorce, which is impossible.
The contradiction is between owning the slave and dissolving the marriage.
2
Understand the Hanafi reasoning for divorce without dower
The word 'khul'' is a kinayah (metaphorical expression) for divorce. The dower indicates intent, so the divorce occurs. The contradiction is resolved by dropping the dower, as wealth is not essential for divorce.
This is compared to khul' with an irrevocably divorced woman (mubannah): divorce occurs, but the dower is not obligatory.
3
Examine Abu Yusuf's opposing view
Abu Yusuf analogizes to a free woman marrying a slave on his neck with the master's permission: that marriage is entirely void. He argues the same applies to khul' because the condition contradictory to marriage also contradicts its dissolution.
Abu Yusuf's analogy is from the formation of marriage, not its dissolution.
4
Apply the 'lesser contradiction' principle
The apparent view (zahir al-riwayah) is that removing the contradiction is obligatory by dropping the lesser element, which is the dower. The divorce is retained because it is the primary purpose.
This is the preferred view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, adopted by the majority of the Hanafi school.
5
Contrast with the slave husband case
If the husband is a slave (qinn, mukatab, mudabbar), the khul' is valid with the named dower because the husband does not own his own neck; ownership goes to his master, so no contradiction arises.
This shows that the obstacle is only when the husband would become owner of the dower.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ولو خالع الخ ) رجل زوج أمته من رجل ودخل بها الزوج فخالع السيد الأمة من زوجها على رقبتها، فإن كان الزوج حراً لا يصح الخلع في حق البدل وإلا لملك الزوج رقبتها مقارناً لوقوع الطلاق وذلك منافٍ له لأنه متى صح الخلع ملك الزوج رقبتها فيبطل النكاح فيبطل الخلع لكنها تبين بطلقة لأنه لم يمكن تصحيحه خلعاً بقي لفظ الخلع وهو من الكنايات ولا يحتاج إلى نية لدلالة البدل على الطلاق. وعن أبي يوسف : لا أيضاً اعتباراً بما لو تزوج على رقبته بإذن المولى حرة حيث بطل النكاح أصلاً لبطلان التسمية لأن الشرط المنافي للنكاح منافٍ للطلاق ضرورة إذ لا يتصور رفع النكاح حال عدمه. ووجه الظاهر أن إسقاط المنافاة واجب وذلك بإسقاط أدنى المتنافيين وهو هنا المسمى لأن المال زائد في الطلاق لصحته بدونه فكان أولى بالرد من الطلاق كما لو خلع مبانته على ألف يقع الطلاق، ولا يجب المال بخلاف النكاح لأنه لم يشرع بغير مال وقد تعذر إيجاب المال أصلاً لأن تسمية السيد رقبة الأمة بدلاً في الخلع صحيحة لكون الرقبة مالاً متقوماً وصحة التسمية تنفي وجوب مهر المثل، والمنافاة تنفي وجوب قيمة المسمى لأن المصير إليها من قضايا فساد تسمية لا تقتضي بطلان النكاح ولو تحققت كما في مهر يصح الطلاق. قوله : (وكذا لو طلقها الخ وكذا لا يصح إيجاب البدل لو طلقها الزوج على رقبتها إلا أنه هنا يقع رجعياً لأنه صريح قوله : ( ولو كان أي الزوج رقيقاً قناً أو مكاتباً أو مدبراً صح الخلع بالمسمى لما مر من عدم المانع وهو ملك أحد الزوجين رقبة الآخر لأن الملك يقع للمولى قوله : (ولو خلعهما الخ . أمتان زينب وعمرة فخلعهما سيدهما على رقبة عمرة مثلاً صح في حق التي لم يعينها للبدل وهي زينب فتطلق بحصتها من رقبة عمرة إذا قسمت رقبتها على قدر مهر مثلهما المسمى، فما أصاب مهر زينب فللزوج، وما أصاب مهر عمرة بقي للمولي . وإنما صح الخلع في حق زينب لأنه أمكن تصحيحه لأن
His statement: (And if he makes khul' etc.) A man married his slave woman to a man, and the husband consummated the marriage. Then the master made khul' of the slave woman from her husband on her neck (i.e., using her own person as the dower). If the husband is free, the khul' is not valid regarding the dower, otherwise the husband would own her neck simultaneously with the occurrence of divorce, and that is contradictory. For when khul' is valid, the husband owns her neck, which nullifies the marriage, which then nullifies the khul'. However, she becomes irrevocably divorced with one divorce, because since it was not possible to validate it as khul', the word khul' remains, and it is among the kinayah (metaphorical) words, and it does not require intention because the dower indicates divorce. From Abu Yusuf: neither does he consider it valid, by analogy to a free woman marrying on the neck of a slave with the master's permission, where the marriage is entirely invalid due to the nullity of the dower, because the condition contradictory to marriage is necessarily contradictory to divorce, since divorce cannot be imagined when there is no marriage. The apparent view is that removing the contradiction is obligatory by dropping the lesser of the two contradictory elements, which here is the dower, because wealth is superfluous in divorce (since divorce is valid without it), so it is more deserving of being returned than the divorce, just as if a man made khul' with his irrevocably divorced wife for a thousand, the divorce occurs and the wealth is not obligatory. Unlike marriage, which was not legislated without wealth, and it became impossible to obligate the wealth entirely because the master's naming of the slave woman's neck as a dower in khul' is valid since the neck is valuable property, and the validity of the naming negates the obligation of fair dower, and the contradiction negates the obligation of the value of the named item, because resorting to it is from the consequences of corrupt naming, which does not necessitate nullification of the marriage, even if it occurred, as in the case of a dower that makes the divorce valid. His statement: (And likewise if he divorces her etc.) And likewise, it is not valid to impose the dower if the husband divorces her on her neck, except that here it occurs as a revocable divorce because it is explicit. His statement: (And if the husband is a slave, whether a qinn, mukatab, or mudabbar, the khul' is valid for the named dower) as mentioned, because there is no obstacle, which is the ownership of one spouse over the other's neck, because the ownership falls to the master. His statement: (And if he makes khul' with both of them etc.) Two slave women, Zaynab and Amrah; their master made khul' with both of them on the neck of Amrah, for example. It is valid regarding the one not specified as the dower, i.e., Zaynab, and she is divorced for her share from the neck of Amrah, if her neck is divided according to the named fair dowers of the two. Then what corresponds to Zaynab's dower goes to the husband, and what corresponds to Amrah's dower remains with the master. And it is valid regarding Zaynab because it was possible to validate it because...
Source pages p. 493

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the ruling of khul' (divorce by mutual consent with compensation) using the slave woman's own neck as the dower. The commentary addresses several scenarios. First, if the master makes khul' of his slave woman from her free husband on her neck, the khul' is invalid regarding the dower because the husband would simultaneously own the slave woman (the dower) and divorce her, which is contradictory. However, the word 'khul'' used is a kinayah (metaphorical expression) for divorce, and since the dower indicates the intent, the divorce still takes effect as a single irrevocable divorce (bainunah sughra) without the dower being owed. Abu Yusuf disagrees, analogizing to a free woman marrying a slave on his neck with master's permission, where the marriage is entirely invalid. The preferred view (zahir al-riwayah) is that the contradiction is resolved by dropping the dower, as wealth is not essential for divorce. If the husband is a slave (qinn, mukatab, or mudabbar), the khul' is valid with the named dower, because the husband does not own his own neck; ownership goes to his master. Finally, the case of two slave women: if the master makes khul' with both on the neck of one, it is valid for the one not specified as the dower, and she is divorced for her proportional share of the neck value.

Key Terms
خلع على الرقبة (khul' 'ala al-raqabah): A divorce by mutual consent where the compensation is the slave woman's own person (neck), often used in cases of slave wives.
رقبة (raqabah): Literally 'neck', but in legal context refers to the slave's entire person as property; used as dower or compensation in khul'.
مبانة (mubannah): An irrevocably divorced woman (bainunah sughra or kubra); here used for a woman already divorced irrevocably, making khul' valid without dower.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, if a free husband makes khul' with his slave wife on her neck (using herself as the dower), what is the outcome?
A) The khul' is valid and the husband owes the value of her neck.
B) The khul' is invalid and the marriage continues.
C) The khul' is invalid as a contract, but the wife becomes irrevocably divorced without dower.
D) The khul' is valid only if the husband is a slave.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a free husband's khul' using the slave wife's own neck as dower fails regarding the dower but the divorce still occurs irrevocably.

Obligation of 'Iddah When a Man Buys His Slave Wife
Arguments about 'iddah when a man buys his slave wife

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on 'iddah for a slave wife purchased by her husband and distinguish the obligation between the buyer and third parties.

Core Idea
The buyer's ownership prevents 'iddah from being due to him; for others, it depends on transmission.

When a man buys his own slave wife, does she have to observe 'iddah? Some scholars argue that no 'iddah is needed because ownership overrides marriage. This lesson examines the Hanafi position and the reasoning behind it, highlighting a nuanced distinction between the buyer and third parties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
Determine whether a woman who is both a slave and wife must observe 'iddah when her husband becomes her owner by purchase.
The key is the intersection of marriage and ownership.
2
Analyze the opposing argument
Opponents claim that if 'iddah were obligatory, the master could not marry her to another during it, but they say he can. Note their evidence.
This argument relies on a hypothetical marriage to a third party.
3
Examine the Hanafi response
The Hanafi position rejects the opposition's premise: marrying her to another is not permissible. Thus 'iddah does exist.
The rebuttal shows that the marriage prohibition actually confirms 'iddah.
4
Apply the ownership distinction
Understand that 'iddah is not due to the buyer because he owns her, but for others (e.g., if she were sold to another) it depends on two narrations.
This two-tiered ruling is unique to Hanafi jurisprudence.
5
Appreciate the thamarah al-khilaf
If no 'iddah, she can be married immediately; if 'iddah, she must wait. This affects her legal capacity to contract marriage.
The practical consequence (thamarah) is the critical takeaway for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا دليل على وجوب العدة قلنا : قد قالوا إنه لا عدة عليها بدليل أنه لو زوجها من آخر جاز، والصحيح أنه لا يجوز تزويجها من آخر. والحاصل أنه لا تجب العدة عليها في حق من اشتراها، وهل تجب في حق غيره فهو على الروايتين ا هـ .
And this is evidence for the obligation of 'iddah. We said: They have said that there is no 'iddah upon her, evidenced by the fact that if he married her to another, it would be permissible. But the correct view is that it is not permissible to marry her to another. The conclusion is that 'iddah is not obligatory upon her with respect to the one who bought her. As to whether it is obligatory with respect to others, that depends on two narrations.
Source pages p. 494

Commentary
This passage discusses the obligation of 'iddah when a man buys his own slave wife. Some argued that no 'iddah is required because if the master married her off to another, the marriage would be valid, implying no 'iddah. The correct Hanafi position is that such a marriage is invalid (there is 'iddah). However, the 'iddah is not owed to the buyer himself (since she is his property), but it may be owed to others according to two narrations. This distinction is crucial: the buyer's ownership prevents 'iddah from being due to him, but it may be due to others.

Key Terms
عدة ('iddah): The waiting period after divorce or death before a woman can remarry.
ملك اليمين (milk al-yamin): Ownership by right hand possession, i.e., owning a slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Hanafi ruling regarding 'iddah for a man who buys his own slave wife?
A) 'Iddah is obligatory upon her with respect to the buyer.
B) 'Iddah is not obligatory upon her with respect to the buyer.
C) 'Iddah is always obligatory regardless of ownership.
D) 'Iddah is only obligatory if she was divorced before the purchase.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when a man buys his slave wife, 'iddah is not due to the buyer, but may be due to others based on two narrations.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
8 min
Effect of Purchase After Pronouncing Talaq: Muhammad vs. Abu Yusuf
Intra-Hanafi analysis of divorce after purchase and emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether a divorce pronounced before purchase can take effect after emancipation, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Ownership prevents divorce; once a divorce falls, it cannot return — two principles in conflict.

Ownership (milk al-yamin) is a barrier to the effectiveness of divorce. But what if a husband pronounces talaq while his wife is owned by him, and then later sells or emancipates her? Does the divorce spring back to life? Imam Muhammad and Imam Abu Yusuf differed on this, and their disagreement is rooted in two conflicting legal maxims. This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents the arguments and identifies the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core scenario
A husband pronounces talaq while his wife is his slave (or he owns her). Then he purchases her (or she becomes free). Does the divorce take effect after the ownership is removed?
2
State the two Hanafi positions
Imam Muhammad holds that divorce takes effect after ownership is removed (e.g., emancipation). Abu Yusuf holds that it does not, because once the divorce fell (sāqaṭa), it cannot return.
Both imams agree on the basic principle: ownership prevents effectiveness.
3
Analyze each proof
Muhammad's proof: The divorce was uttered and the only barrier was ownership; once the barrier is gone, the divorce applies. Abu Yusuf's proof: The divorce is like a debt that was cancelled; cancellation cannot be reversed.
Abu Yusuf relies on the maxim 'al-sāqiṭ lā ya'ūd'.
4
Apply to the variations
Same reasoning applies if the divorce was conditional on a future event or if it was a sunna divorce. The passage also distinguishes between a free husband (agreement) and a slave husband (fatwa on Abu Yusuf's view).
5
Identify the mufta bihi
In the case of a slave husband, the fatwa is on Abu Yusuf's view. For a free husband, all agree the divorce does not fall after purchase because ownership never changed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهكذا في المعراج قيد بشرائه لأنها لو ملكته أو شقصاً منه ثم طلقها وقع فيما روي عن محمد، ولا فرق بينهما في ظاهر الرواية عن الكل لأن العدة وإن وجبت لكن ملك اليمين مانع من مالكية الطلاق. وأطلق الشراء وأراد الملك مجازاً، وقيد بكون الطلاق وهي مملوكة له لأنه لو أعتقها بعد الملك ثم طلقها وهي في العدة وقع الطلاق عليه لزوال المانع من ظهور العدة وهو الملك، وكذا لو أعتقته ما ملكته ثم طلقها وقع طلاقه عند محمد لزوال المنافي لمالكية الطلاق ولهذا يجب عليه النفقة والسكني، ولم يقع عند أبي يوسف فيهما لأن الساقط لا يعود. ولو علق طلاقها بشرط أو قال أنت طالق للسنة أو آلى منها قبل الشراء فوجد الشرط أو جاء وقت السنة أو مضت مدة الإيلاء بعد الشراء والعتق وقع عليها الطلاق، وإن وجد ذلك بعد الشراء قبل العتق لم يقع في الوجهين، والبيع بعد الشراء كالعتق فيما ذكرنا لزوال المانع . كذا ذكر الشارح. وفي الولوالجية : عبد قال لامرأته الحرة أنت طالق للسنة فاشترته وقع عليها الطلاق إذا طهرت في قياس قول محمد وعلى قياس قول أبي يوسف لا يقع عليها وعليه الفتوى. والحر لو قال لامرأته ذلك ثم اشتراها لم يقع الطلاق اتفاقاً لأنه لم يبق الملك ا هـ .
Similarly in al-Mi'raj, it is restricted to his purchase because if she owned him or a share of him then he divorced, it falls according to what is narrated from Muhammad, and there is no difference between the two in the apparent narration from all, because even if 'iddah is obligatory, however the right hand possession prevents the effectiveness of divorce. And he used the word purchase but intended ownership metaphorically, and the divorce is restricted to when she is owned by him because if he emancipated her after ownership and then divorced her while she is in 'iddah, the divorce falls upon him because of the removal of the preventative of the appearance of 'iddah, which is ownership. Similarly, if she emancipated him while he owned her, then he divorces her, his divorce falls according to Muhammad because of the removal of what contradicts the effectiveness of divorce, and for this reason maintenance and housing are obligatory upon him. But it does not fall according to Abu Yusuf in both cases because what falls does not return. And if he made divorce conditional on a condition, or said 'You are divorced according to the sunna', or took an oath of abstinence before purchase, then the condition occurs or the time of sunna comes or the period of ila' passes after purchase and emancipation, the divorce falls upon her. And if that occurs after purchase but before emancipation, it does not fall in both cases. And sale after purchase is like emancipation in what we mentioned due to removal of the preventative. Thus mentioned by the commentator. And in al-Walwalijiyya: A slave man said to his free wife 'You are divorced according to the sunna', then she purchased him; the divorce falls upon her when she becomes pure according to the qiyas of Muhammad's view, and according to the qiyas of Abu Yusuf's view it does not fall upon her, and on that is the fatwa. And a free man if he said that to his wife and then purchased her, the divorce does not fall by agreement because he no longer has ownership.
Source pages p. 494

Commentary
This passage analyzes the effect of purchase or emancipation after pronouncing divorce. The key principle: ownership (milk al-yamin) prevents the effectiveness of divorce (man' min mālkiyya al-talāq). When a husband buys his slave wife, the ownership is established, and divorce pronounced before purchase is nullified. However, if he subsequently emancipates her, ownership is removed, and the divorce can now take effect according to Muhammad. Abu Yusuf disagrees, arguing that what has fallen (sāqiṭ) does not return. The same applies to conditional divorce: if the condition occurs after ownership is removed (by purchase and then emancipation), the divorce falls. Different rulings apply for slave vs free husbands. The fatwa is on Abu Yusuf's view in the case of a slave husband.

Key Terms
مالكية الطلاق (mālikiyyat al-ṭalāq): The effectiveness or legal power of divorce to take effect.
الساقط لا يعود (al-sāqiṭ lā ya'ūd): A legal maxim meaning that what has fallen (become null) does not return.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling when a free man says to his wife 'You are divorced according to the sunna' and then purchases her?
A) The divorce falls by agreement
B) The divorce does not fall by agreement because he no longer has ownership
C) It falls only according to Muhammad
D) It falls only according to Abu Yusuf

Key Takeaway
When ownership prevents divorce from taking effect, the removal of ownership does not necessarily revive the divorce; the fatwa follows Abu Yusuf's maxim 'al-sāqiṭ lā ya'ūd' for slave husbands.

Talaq — Divorce

Conditional Divorce When Husband Inherits Slave Wife: The Concurrent vs Sequential Debate
Intra-Hanafi dispute on conditional divorce and inheritance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on conditional divorce upon inheritance and explain the qiyas-based reasoning behind each view.

Core Idea
Ownership and divorce are mutually exclusive; concurrence vs sequence determines validity.

This lesson examines a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute on conditional divorce. When a husband marries his testator’s slave and conditions divorce upon her master’s death, does the divorce occur if the husband inherits her? The debate between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hinges on whether the events are concurrent or sequential, with each using the same water-pot analogy to defend their position.

Guided Path
1
State the factual scenario
Identify who says what: a man marries the slave of his testator, then says 'If your master dies, you are twice divorced.' The master dies, and the husband is heir.
2
Identify the two Hanafi positions
Abu Yusuf holds that divorce occurs; Muhammad holds that neither divorce nor emancipation occurs (after retraction).
Muhammad initially said emancipation, then retracted.
3
Analyze the reasoning of each imam
Muhammad: ownership and divorce are opposites; they cannot coexist, so divorce is attached to the termination of marriage, which cannot happen concurrently with acquisition. Abu Yusuf: events are sequential—first ownership, then termination—so divorce is possible.
4
Examine the water-pot qiyas
Both use the analogy of a pot full of water: when a stone is thrown in, water exits. Muhammad says exit is concurrent with entry; Abu Yusuf says it is sequential.
The pot cannot hold both stone and water simultaneously—this is the key illa.
5
Note the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
If Muhammad’s view prevails, the wife is not divorced and remains married; if Abu Yusuf’s, she is divorced and must observe iddah.
The dispute also extends to conditional emancipation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من والده ثم تزوجها ثم قال لها إذا مات والدي فأنت طالق ثنتين فمات الوالد كان محمد يقول أولاً تعتق ولا تطلق ثم رجع وقال : لا يقع طلاق ولا عتاق، والمسألة على استقصاء في المبسوط ا هـ . وفي المحيط من باب ما تحل به المطلقة : ولو تزوج أمة مورثه قال لها إذا مات مولاك فأنت طالق ثنتين ثم مات المولى والزوج وارثه يقع الطلاق عند أبي يوسف، وعند محمد لا تطلق لأن الطلاق مضاف إلى حال زوال النكاح لأن الوارث يملك الأمة مقارناً لزوالها عن ملك الميت وزوال النكاح يثبت مقارناً بدخولها في ملك الزوج لأن هذه أشياء متضادة متنافية وملك اليمين يضاد ملك النكاح في حق وثمراتها وثبوت أحد الضدين يكون مقارناً لذهاب الضد الآخر لا مرتباً عليه كثبوت السواد يكون مقارناً لذهاب البياض، وكقدح مملوء من الماء إذا ألقي فيه حجر وخرج الماء يكون خروج الماء مقارناً لدخول الحجر لا مرتباً عليه لاستحالة أن يكون القدح واسعاً للحجر ثم يخرج الماء بعده وإضافة الطلاق إلى حال زوال النكاح لا يصح لأبي يوسف أن الطلاق مضاف إلى حال قيام النكاح لأن زوال النكاح يترتب على ملك الوارث، وملك الوارث يترتب على انقطاع ملك الميت وهذه أحوال متعاقبة مترادفة لأن القول بالمقارنة يؤدي إلى وهو سبق ثبوت الحكم على العلة والحكم لا يثبت إلا بعد تمام العلة فالشراء ما لم يتم لا يزول ملك البائع ولا يدخل في ملك المشتري، وهكذا نقول في قدح الماء يترتب خروج الماء على دخول الحجر ولا يقترنان لاستحالة إنبات الخروج قبل دخول الحجر الذي علة الخروج. وعلى هذا لو قال لأمة مورثه إذا مات مولاك فأنت حرة فمات المولى لا هو استحالة ن وقال زفر وهو رواية عن محمد تعتق لأن موت المورث سبب لملك الوارث فقد تعتق، أضافه إلى سبب الملك فصح كما لو قال إن ورثتك ولنا أن شرط العتق وهو الموت وجد حالة انقطاع ملك الميت لا حال قيام ملك الوارث فيكون ملك الحالف بعد العتق بساعتين فلا يكون العتق مضافاً إلى الملك ولا إلى سبب الملك لأن الموت لم يوضع سبباً لإفادة ملك ملکه هو القرابة بعد الموت، وأما إذا جمع بين اليمين بالطلاق والعتاق بأن الوارث بل سبب قال إن مات مولاك فأنت طالق ثنتين قال محمد لا يقعان وقال أبو يوسف بالطلاق فقط.
From his father, then he married her, then he said to her: 'If my father dies, you are twice divorced.' The father died. Muhammad initially said: She is emancipated and not divorced, then he retracted and said: Neither divorce nor emancipation occurs. The case is detailed in al-Mabsut. And in al-Muhit from the chapter 'What Makes the Divorced Woman Permissible': If he married the slave woman of his testator and said: 'If your master dies, you are twice divorced,' then the master died and the husband is his heir, divorce occurs according to Abu Yusuf, but according to Muhammad she is not divorced because the divorce is attached to the state of termination of marriage, because the heir owns the slave girl concurrent with her leaving the ownership of the deceased, and the termination of marriage is established concurrently with her entering the husband's ownership. These things are contradictory and mutually exclusive: right-hand possession contradicts marriage ownership in rights and effects. The establishment of one opposite occurs concurrent with the disappearance of the other, not sequentially – like the establishment of black being concurrent with the disappearance of white. And like a pot full of water: if a stone is thrown in and water comes out, the exit of water is concurrent with the entry of the stone, not sequential, because it is impossible for the pot to accommodate the stone and then the water leaves afterwards. Attaching divorce to the state of termination of marriage is invalid. According to Abu Yusuf, divorce is attached to the state of existence of marriage because the termination of marriage is dependent on the heir's ownership, and the heir's ownership is dependent on the cessation of the deceased's ownership. These are successive, sequential states, because the theory of concurrence leads to... and the ruling is not established except after the complete cause. So in the water pot, the exit of water is sequential upon the entry of the stone, not concurrent. On this basis, if he said to his testator's slave girl: 'If your master dies, you are free,' then the master died, ... and Zufar, and it is a narration from Muhammad, says: She becomes free because the death of the testator is a cause for the heir's ownership, so she becomes free. He attached it to the cause of ownership, so it is valid, as if he said 'If you inherit me.' But we hold that the condition of emancipation, which is death, occurs in the state of cessation of the deceased's ownership, not in the state of establishment of the heir's ownership. So the owner of the swearer is two hours after emancipation, so the emancipation is not attached to ownership nor to the cause of ownership. If he combined the oath of divorce and emancipation, saying 'If your master dies, you are twice divorced,' Muhammad says neither occurs, and Abu Yusuf says only divorce occurs.
Source pages p. 495

Commentary
This is a complex dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding the validity of conditional divorce when the condition (death of mawla) coincides with the husband inheriting the slave wife. Muhammad argues that ownership and divorce are mutually exclusive and cannot coexist, so the divorce does not occur because it is attached to the termination of marriage (which cannot happen when ownership enters). Abu Yusuf argues that the events are sequential: first ownership transfers to the heir, then the marriage terminates, so the divorce can be attached to the state before termination. They use the analogy of water and stone: Muhammad says the stone's entry and water's exit are concurrent; Abu Yusuf says the water exits after the stone enters. This has practical consequences for inheritance and divorce. Zufar and a narration from Muhammad hold that for emancipation, it may be valid because death is a cause for ownership.

Key Terms
مقارنة (muqārana): Concurrence or simultaneity of two events.
ترتب (tarattub): Sequential order, one event following another.
مولى (mawla): Master (owner of slave).
عدة (iddah): Waiting period after divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the key reason Muhammad al-Shaybani holds that conditional divorce does not occur when the husband inherits the slave wife?
A) The husband did not intend divorce.
B) Ownership and marriage are opposites and cannot coexist concurrently.
C) The death of the mawla invalidates the marriage automatically.
D) Abu Yusuf's view is stronger.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has a nuanced dispute on whether conditional divorce is valid when the condition coincides with the husband inheriting the slave wife, based on the theoretical concept of concurrence vs. sequence.
